AliceТs Adventures in Wonderland



		All in the golden afternoon
		Full leisurely we glide;[1 - ѕо легенде, „арльз Ћютвидж ƒоджсон (более известный под псевдонимом Ћьюис  эрролл) сочинил историю о приключени€х јлисы в —тране чудес во врем€ катани€ на лодке с преподобным –обинсоном ƒакуортом и трем€ сестрами Ћидделл Ц Ћориной, јлисой и Ёдит. ¬последствии истори€ была немного изменена и опубликована.]

		Beneath such dreamy weather,
		To beg a tale of breath too weak
		To stir the tiniest feather!

		Not more than once a minute.

		In fancy they pursue
		The dreamchild moving through a land
		Of wonders wild and new,
		In friendly chat with bird or beast Ч
		And half believe it true.

		СThe rest next time Ц Т СIt is next time!Т

		Thus slowly, one by one,
		Its quaint events were hammered out Ч

		Alice! A childish story take,
		And with a gentle hand,
		Lay it where ChildhoodТs dreams are twined
		In MemoryТs mystic band,
		Like pilgrimТs witherТd wreath of flowers
		Plucked in a far off land.




Chapter I

Down the Rabbit-Hole


Alice was beginning to get very tired of sitting by her sister on the bank, and of having nothing to do: once or twice she had peeped into the book her sister was reading, but it had no pictures or conversations in it, Сand what is the use of a book,Т thought Alice Сwithout pictures or conversation?Т

So she was considering in her own mind (as well as she could, for the hot day made her feel very sleepy and stupid), whether the pleasure of making a daisychain would be worth the trouble of getting up and picking the daisies, when suddenly a White Rabbit with pink eyes ran close by her.

There was nothing so VERY remarkable in that; nor did Alice think it so VERY much out of the way to hear the Rabbit say to itself, СOh dear! Oh dear! I shall be late!Т (when she thought it over afterwards, it occurred to her that she ought to have wondered at this, but at the time it all seemed quite natural); but when the Rabbit actually TOOK A WATCH OUT OF ITS WAISTCOATPOCKET, and looked at it, and then hurried on, Alice started to her feet, for it flashed across her mind that she had never before seen a rabbit with either a waistcoatpocket, or a watch to take out of it, and burning with curiosity, she ran across the field after it, and fortunately was just in time to see it pop down a large rabbithole under the hedge.

In another moment down went Alice after it, never once considering how in the world she was to get out again.

The rabbithole went straight on like a tunnel for some way, and then dipped suddenly down, so suddenly that Alice had not a moment to think about stopping herself before she found herself falling down a very deep well.

Either the well was very deep, or she fell very slowly, for she had plenty of time as she went down to look about her and to wonder what was going to happen next. First, she tried to look down and make out what she was coming to, but it was too dark to see anything; then she looked at the sides of the well, and noticed that they were filled with cupboards and bookshelves; here and there she saw maps and pictures hung upon pegs. She took down a jar from one of the shelves as she passed; it was labelled СORANGE MARMALADEТ[2 - ORANGE MARMALADE Ц мармелад Ц это варенье, приготовленное из цитрусовых (обычно из апельсинов).], but to her great disappointment it was empty: she did not like to drop the jar for fear of killing somebody, so managed to put it into one of the cupboards as she fell past it.

СWell!Т thought Alice to herself, Сafter such a fall as this, I shall think nothing of tumbling down stairs! How brave theyТll all think me at home! Why, I wouldnТt say anything about it, even if I fell off the top of the house!Т (Which was very likely true.)

Down, down, down. Would the fall NEVER come to an end! СI wonder how many miles[3 - Miles Ц мили, британские единицы измерени€ рассто€ни€. 1 мил€ = 1609 км.] IТve fallen by this time?Т she said aloud. СI must be getting somewhere near the centre of the earth. Let me see: that would be four thousand miles down, I thinkЦТ (for, you see, Alice had learnt several things of this sort in her lessons in the schoolroom[4 - Schoolroom Ц в ¬икторианскую эпоху дети получали основы образовани€ дома, занима€сь в классной комнате с гувернантками или гувернерами. «атем мальчики отправл€лись в школыпансионы, а девочки часто продолжали обучение дома.], and though this was not a VERY good opportunity for showing off her knowledge, as there was no one to listen to her, still it was good practice to say it over) СЦyes, thatТs about the right distance Ц but then I wonder what Latitude or Longitude IТve got to?Т (Alice had no idea what Latitude was, or Longitude either, but thought they were nice grand words to say.)

Presently she began again. СI wonder if I shall fall right THROUGH the earth! How funny itТll seem to come out among the people that walk with their heads downward! The Antipathies, I thinkЦТ (she was rather glad there WAS no one listening, this time, as it didnТt sound at all the right word) СЦbut I shall have to ask them what the name of the country is, you know. Please, MaТam, is this New Zealand or Australia?Т (and she tried to curtsey as she spoke Ц fancy CURTSEYING as youТre falling through the air! Do you think you could manage it?) СAnd what an ignorant little girl sheТll think me for asking! No, itТll never do to ask: perhaps I shall see it written up somewhere.Т

Down, down, down. There was nothing else to do, so Alice soon began talking again. СDinahТll miss me very much tonight, I should think!Т (Dinah was the cat.) СI hope theyТll remember her saucer of milk at teatime. Dinah my dear! I wish you were down here with me! There are no mice in the air, IТm afraid, but you might catch a bat, and thatТs very like a mouse, you know. But do cats eat bats, I wonder?Т And here Alice began to get rather sleepy, and went on saying to herself, in a dreamy sort of way, СDo cats eat bats? Do cats eat bats?Т and sometimes, СDo bats eat cats?Т for, you see, as she couldnТt answer either question, it didnТt much matter which way she put it. She felt that she was dozing off, and had just begun to dream that she was walking hand in hand with Dinah, and saying to her very earnestly, СNow, Dinah, tell me the truth: did you ever eat a bat?Т when suddenly, thump! thump! down she came upon a heap of sticks and dry leaves, and the fall was over.

Alice was not a bit hurt, and she jumped up on to her feet in a moment: she looked up, but it was all dark overhead; before her was another long passage, and the White Rabbit was still in sight, hurrying down it. There was not a moment to be lost: away went Alice like the wind, and was just in time to hear it say, as it turned a corner, СOh my ears and whiskers, how late itТs getting!Т She was close behind it when she turned the corner, but the Rabbit was no longer to be seen: she found herself in a long, low hall, which was lit up by a row of lamps hanging from the roof.

There were doors all round the hall, but they were all locked; and when Alice had been all the way down one side and up the other, trying every door, she walked sadly down the middle, wondering how she was ever to get out again.

Suddenly she came upon a little threelegged table, all made of solid glass; there was nothing on it except a tiny golden key, and AliceТs first thought was that it might belong to one of the doors of the hall; but, alas! either the locks were too large, or the key was too small, but at any rate it would not open any of them. However, on the second time round, she came upon a low curtain she had not noticed before, and behind it was a little door about fifteen inches[5 - Inch Ц дюйм, единица измерени€ длины. 1 дюйм Ц 2,54 см.] high: she tried the little golden key in the lock, and to her great delight it fitted!

Alice opened the door and found that it led into a small passage, not much larger than a rathole: she knelt down and looked along the passage into the loveliest garden you ever saw. How she longed to get out of that dark hall, and wander about among those beds of bright flowers and those cool fountains, but she could not even get her head though the doorway; Сand even if my head would go through,Т thought poor Alice, Сit would be of very little use without my shoulders. Oh, how I wish I could shut up like a telescope! I think I could, if I only know how to begin.Т For, you see, so many outoftheway things had happened lately, that Alice had begun to think that very few things indeed were really impossible.

There seemed to be no use in waiting by the little door, so she went back to the table, half hoping she might find another key on it, or at any rate a book of rules for shutting people up like telescopes: this time she found a little bottle on it, (Сwhich certainly was not here before,Т said Alice,) and round the neck of the bottle was a paper label, with the words СDRINK MEТ beautifully printed on it in large letters.

It was all very well to say СDrink me,Т but the wise little Alice was not going to do THAT in a hurry. СNo, IТll look first,Т she said, Сand see whether itТs marked УpoisonФ or notТ; for she had read several nice little histories about children who had got burnt, and eaten up by wild beasts and other unpleasant things, all because they WOULD not remember the simple rules their friends had taught them: such as, that a redhot poker will burn you if you hold it too long; and that if you cut your finger VERY deeply with a knife, it usually bleeds; and she had never forgotten that, if you drink much from a bottle marked Сpoison,Т it is almost certain to disagree with you, sooner or later.

However, this bottle was NOT marked Сpoison,Т so Alice ventured to taste it, and finding it very nice, (it had, in fact, a sort of mixed flavour of cherrytart, custard, pineapple, roast turkey, toffee, and hot buttered toast,) she very soon finished it off.


* * *

СWhat a curious feeling!Т said Alice; СI must be shutting up like a telescope.Т

And so it was indeed: she was now only ten inches high, and her face brightened up at the thought that she was now the right size for going through the little door into that lovely garden. First, however, she waited for a few minutes to see if she was going to shrink any further: she felt a little nervous about this; Сfor it might end, you know,Т said Alice to herself, Сin my going out altogether, like a candle. I wonder what I should be like then?Т And she tried to fancy what the flame of a candle is like after the candle is blown out, for she could not remember ever having seen such a thing.

After a while, finding that nothing more happened, she decided on going into the garden at once; but, alas for poor Alice! when she got to the door, she found she had forgotten the little golden key, and when she went back to the table for it, she found she could not possibly reach it: she could see it quite plainly through the glass, and she tried her best to climb up one of the legs of the table, but it was too slippery; and when she had tired herself out with trying, the poor little thing sat down and cried.

СCome, thereТs no use in crying like that!Т said Alice to herself, rather sharply; СI advise you to leave off this minute!Т She generally gave herself very good advice, (though she very seldom followed it), and sometimes she scolded herself so severely as to bring tears into her eyes; and once she remembered trying to box her own ears for having cheated herself in a game of croquet she was playing against herself, for this curious child was very fond of pretending to be two people. СBut itТs no use now,Т thought poor Alice, Сto pretend to be two people! Why, thereТs hardly enough of me left to make ONE respectable person!Т

Soon her eye fell on a little glass box that was lying under the table: she opened it, and found in it a very small cake, on which the words СEAT MEТ were beautifully marked in currants[6 - —urrants Ц сушеный виноград сорта Ђкоринкаї Ц мелкий, сладкий и без косточек. ≈го часто кладут в выпечку.]. СWell, IТll eat it,Т said Alice, Сand if it makes me grow larger, I can reach the key; and if it makes me grow smaller, I can creep under the door; so either way IТll get into the garden, and I donТt care which happens!Т

She ate a little bit, and said anxiously to herself, СWhich way? Which way?Т, holding her hand on the top of her head to feel which way it was growing, and she was quite surprised to find that she remained the same size: to be sure, this generally happens when one eats cake, but Alice had got so much into the way of expecting nothing but outoftheway things to happen, that it seemed quite dull and stupid for life to go on in the common way.




Chapter II

The Pool of Tears


СCuriouser[7 - —uriouser Ц эта форма некорректна: так как слово curious состоит из более чем двух слогов, правильна€ сравнительна€ степень будет more curious. ¬озможно, использование такой формы Ц это кака€то шутка, известна€ в семействе Ћидделл. ј может быть, это Ђдетское словечкої Ц просто распространенна€ ошибка, которую делают дети.] and curiouser!Т cried Alice (she was so much surprised, that for the moment she quite forgot how to speak good English); Сnow IТm opening out like the largest telescope that ever was! Goodbye, feet!Т (for when she looked down at her feet, they seemed to be almost out of sight, they were getting so far off). СOh, my poor little feet, I wonder who will put on your shoes and stockings for you now, dears? IТm sure I shanТt be able! I shall be a great deal too far off to trouble myself about you: you must manage the best way you can; Ц but I must be kind to them,Т thought Alice, Сor perhaps they wonТt walk the way I want to go! Let me see: IТll give them a new pair of boots every Christmas.Т

And she went on planning to herself how she would manage it. СThey must go by the carrier,Т she thought; Сand how funny itТll seem, sending presents to oneТs own feet! And how odd the directions will look!

$$$$$$$$$$$$$$$(WITH ALICEТS LOVE).


Oh dear, what nonsense IТm talking!Т

Just then her head struck against the roof of the hall: in fact she was now more than nine feet[8 - Feet Ц мн.ч. от foot. 1 фут = 30 см.] high, and she at once took up the little golden key and hurried off to the garden door.

Poor Alice! It was as much as she could do, lying down on one side, to look through into the garden with one eye; but to get through was more hopeless than ever: she sat down and began to cry again.

СYou ought to be ashamed of yourself,Т said Alice, Сa great girl like you,Т (she might well say this), Сto go on crying in this way! Stop this moment, I tell you!Т But she went on all the same, shedding gallons[9 - Gallon = 4,55 литра.] of tears, until there was a large pool all round her, about four inches deep and reaching half down the hall.

After a time she heard a little pattering of feet in the distance, and she hastily dried her eyes to see what was coming. It was the White Rabbit returning, splendidly dressed, with a pair of white kid gloves in one hand and a large fan in the other: he came trotting along in a great hurry, muttering to himself as he came, СOh! the Duchess, the Duchess! Oh! wonТt she be savage if IТve kept her waiting!Т Alice felt so desperate that she was ready to ask help of any one; so, when the Rabbit came near her, she began, in a low, timid voice, СIf you please, sirЦТ The Rabbit started violently, dropped the white kid gloves and the fan, and skurried away into the darkness as hard as he could go.

Alice took up the fan and gloves, and, as the hall was very hot, she kept fanning herself all the time she went on talking: СDear, dear! How queer everything is today! And yesterday things went on just as usual. I wonder if IТve been changed in the night? Let me think: was I the same when I got up this morning? I almost think I can remember feeling a little different. But if IТm not the same, the next question is, Who in the world am I? Ah, THATТS the great puzzle!Т And she began thinking over all the children she knew that were of the same age as herself, to see if she could have been changed for any of them.

СIТm sure IТm not Ada,Т she said, Сfor her hair goes in such long ringlets, and mine doesnТt go in ringlets at all; and IТm sure I canТt be Mabel, for I know all sorts of things, and she, oh! she knows such a very little! Besides, SHEТS she, and IТm I, and Ц oh dear, how puzzling it all is! IТll try if I know all the things I used to know. Let me see: four times five is twelve, and four times six is thirteen, and four times seven is Ц oh dear! I shall never get to twenty at that rate![10 - Ёто математическа€ шутка: специалисты утверждают, что, продолжа€ последовательность, до двадцати јлиса действительно не доберетс€.] However, the Multiplication Table doesnТt signify: letТs try Geography. London is the capital of Paris, and Paris is the capital of Rome, and RomeЦno, THATТS all wrong, IТm certain! I must have been changed for Mabel! IТll try and say УHow doth the littleЦФ[11 - УHow doth the littleЦФ Ц это начало нравоучительного стихотворени€ дл€ детей »саака ”оттса (1674Ц1748), которое называетс€ Ђѕротив праздности и озорстваї.]Т and she crossed her hands on her lap as if she were saying lessons, and began to repeat it, but her voice sounded hoarse and strange, and the words did not come the same as they used to do: Ц

		СHow doth the little crocodile
		Improve his shining tail,
		And pour the waters of the Nile
		On every golden scale!

		СHow cheerfully he seems to grin,
		How neatly spread his claws,
		And welcome little fishes in
		With gently smiling jaws!Т

СIТm sure those are not the right words,Т said poor Alice, and her eyes filled with tears again as she went on, СI must be Mabel after all, and I shall have to go and live in that poky little house, and have next to no toys to play with, and oh! ever so many lessons to learn! No, IТve made up my mind about it; if IТm Mabel, IТll stay down here! ItТll be no use their putting their heads down and saying УCome up again, dear!Ф I shall only look up and say УWho am I then? Tell me that first, and then, if I like being that person, IТll come up: if not, IТll stay down here till IТm somebody elseФ Ц but, oh dear!Т cried Alice, with a sudden burst of tears, СI do wish they WOULD put their heads down! I am so VERY tired of being all alone here!Т

As she said this she looked down at her hands, and was surprised to see that she had put on one of the RabbitТs little white kid gloves while she was talking. СHow CAN I have done that?Т she thought. СI must be growing small again.Т She got up and went to the table to measure herself by it, and found that, as nearly as she could guess, she was now about two feet high, and was going on shrinking rapidly: she soon found out that the cause of this was the fan she was holding, and she dropped it hastily, just in time to avoid shrinking away altogether.

СThat WAS a narrow escape!Т said Alice, a good deal frightened at the sudden change, but very glad to find herself still in existence; Сand now for the garden!Т and she ran with all speed back to the little door: but, alas! the little door was shut again, and the little golden key was lying on the glass table as before, Сand things are worse than ever,Т thought the poor child, Сfor I never was so small as this before, never! And I declare itТs too bad, that it is!Т

As she said these words her foot slipped, and in another moment, splash! she was up to her chin in salt water. Her first idea was that she had somehow fallen into the sea, Сand in that case I can go back by railway,Т she said to herself. (Alice had been to the seaside once in her life, and had come to the general conclusion, that wherever you go to on the English coast you find a number of bathing machines[12 - Bathing machines Ц купальные машины: четырехколесные повозки, зат€нутые холстом. ѕереодеватьс€ в купальный костюм нужно было внутри, после чего лошади ввозили повозку в воду и разворачивали ее так, чтобы купальщика не было видно с берега. ѕосле этого можно было спуститьс€ в воду.] in the sea, some children digging in the sand with wooden spades, then a row of lodging houses, and behind them a railway station.) However, she soon made out that she was in the pool of tears which she had wept when she was nine feet high.

СI wish I hadnТt cried so much!Т said Alice, as she swam about, trying to find her way out. СI shall be punished for it now, I suppose, by being drowned in my own tears! That WILL be a queer thing, to be sure! However, everything is queer today.Т

Just then she heard something splashing about in the pool a little way off, and she swam nearer to make out what it was: at first she thought it must be a walrus or hippopotamus, but then she remembered how small she was now, and she soon made out that it was only a mouse that had slipped in like herself.

СWould it be of any use, now,Т thought Alice, Сto speak to this mouse? Everything is so outoftheway down here, that I should think very likely it can talk: at any rate, thereТs no harm in trying.Т So she began: СO Mouse, do you know the way out of this pool? I am very tired of swimming about here, O Mouse!Т (Alice thought this must be the right way of speaking to a mouse: she had never done such a thing before, but she remembered having seen in her brotherТs Latin Grammar, СA mouse Ц of a mouse Ц to a mouse Ц a mouse Ц O mouse![13 - A mouse Ц of a mouse Ц to a mouse Ц a mouse Ц O mouse! Ц Ёто именительный, родительный, дательный, винительный и звательный падежи соответственно.]Т The Mouse looked at her rather inquisitively, and seemed to her to wink with one of its little eyes, but it said nothing.

СPerhaps it doesnТt understand English,Т thought Alice; СI daresay itТs a French mouse, come over with William the Conqueror[14 - William the Conqueror Ц ¬ильгельм «авоеватель, первый норманнский король јнглии (1066Ц1078). ≈го победа в битве при √астингсе (1066) привела его к власти и дала начало нормандскому завоеванию јнглии.].Т (For, with all her knowledge of history, Alice had no very clear notion how long ago anything had happened.) So she began again: СO&#249; est ma chatte?[15 - O&#249; est ma chatte? Ц √де мо€ кошка? (фр.)]Т which was the first sentence in her French lessonbook. The Mouse gave a sudden leap out of the water, and seemed to quiver all over with fright. СOh, I beg your pardon!Т cried Alice hastily, afraid that she had hurt the poor animalТs feelings. СI quite forgot you didnТt like cats.Т

СNot like cats!Т cried the Mouse, in a shrill, passionate voice. СWould YOU like cats if you were me?Т

СWell, perhaps not,Т said Alice in a soothing tone: СdonТt be angry about it. And yet I wish I could show you our cat Dinah: I think youТd take a fancy to cats if you could only see her. She is such a dear quiet thing,Т Alice went on, half to herself, as she swam lazily about in the pool, Сand she sits purring so nicely by the fire, licking her paws and washing her face Ц and she is such a nice soft thing to nurse Ц and sheТs such a capital one for catching mice Ц oh, I beg your pardon!Т cried Alice again, for this time the Mouse was bristling all over, and she felt certain it must be really offended. СWe wonТt talk about her any more if youТd rather not.Т

СWe indeed!Т cried the Mouse, who was trembling down to the end of his tail. СAs if I would talk on such a subject! Our family always HATED cats: nasty, low, vulgar things! DonТt let me hear the name again!Т

СI wonТt indeed!Т said Alice, in a great hurry to change the subject of conversation. СAre youЦare you fondЦofЦof dogs?Т The Mouse did not answer, so Alice went on eagerly: СThere is such a nice little dog near our house I should like to show you! A little brighteyed terrier, you know, with oh, such long curly brown hair! And itТll fetch things when you throw them, and itТll sit up and beg for its dinner, and all sorts of things Ц I canТt remember half of themЦand it belongs to a farmer, you know, and he says itТs so useful, itТs worth a hundred pounds! He says it kills all the rats andЦoh dear!Т cried Alice in a sorrowful tone, СIТm afraid IТve offended it again!Т For the Mouse was swimming away from her as hard as it could go, and making quite a commotion in the pool as it went.

So she called softly after it, СMouse dear! Do come back again, and we wonТt talk about cats or dogs either, if you donТt like them!Т When the Mouse heard this, it turned round and swam slowly back to her: its face was quite pale (with passion, Alice thought), and it said in a low trembling voice, СLet us get to the shore, and then IТll tell you my history, and youТll understand why it is I hate cats and dogs.Т

It was high time to go, for the pool was getting quite crowded with the birds and animals that had fallen into it: there were a Duck and a Dodo, a Lory and an Eaglet,[16 - A Duck and a Dodo, a Lory and an Eaglet Ц список существ напоминает об участниках той лодочной прогулки, во врем€ которой была впервые рассказана истори€ об јлисе. Duck Ц это –обинсон ƒакуорт, Dodo Ц сам „арльз Ћютвидж ƒоджсон (который слегка заикалс€, в том числе произнос€ свое им€), а также сестры јлисы: Lory Ц Ћорина, и Eaglet Ц Ёдит.] and several other curious creatures. Alice led the way, and the whole party swam to the shore.




Chapter III

A Caucus-Race and a Long Tale


They were indeed a queerlooking party that assembled on the bank Ц the birds with draggled feathers, the animals with their fur clinging close to them, and all dripping wet, cross, and uncomfortable.

The first question of course was, how to get dry again: they had a consultation about this, and after a few minutes it seemed quite natural to Alice to find herself talking familiarly with them, as if she had known them all her life. Indeed, she had quite a long argument with the Lory, who at last turned sulky, and would only say, СI am older than you, and must know betterТ; and this Alice would not allow without knowing how old it was, and, as the Lory positively refused to tell its age, there was no more to be said.

At last the Mouse, who seemed to be a person of authority among them, called out, СSit down, all of you, and listen to me! IТLL soon make you dry enough!Т They all sat down at once, in a large ring, with the Mouse in the middle. Alice kept her eyes anxiously fixed on it, for she felt sure she would catch a bad cold if she did not get dry very soon.

СAhem!Т said the Mouse with an important air, Сare you all ready? This is the driest thing I know. Silence all round, if you please! СUgh!Т said the Lory, with a shiver.

СNot I!Т said the Lory hastily.

СWhat I was going to say,Т said the Dodo in an offended tone, Сwas, that the best thing to get us dry would be a Caucusrace[18 - Caucusrace Ц caucus означает группу ответственных лиц Ц членов политической партии или собрани€ дл€ обсуждени€ и утверждени€ планов. ƒальнейшее описание отражает отношение автора к подобным видам де€тельности.].Т

СWhat IS a Caucusrace?Т said Alice; not that she wanted much to know, but the Dodo had paused as if it thought that SOMEBODY ought to speak, and no one else seemed inclined to say anything.

СWhy,Т said the Dodo, Сthe best way to explain it is to do it.Т (And, as you might like to try the thing yourself, some winter day, I will tell you how the Dodo managed it.)

First it marked out a racecourse, in a sort of circle, (Сthe exact shape doesnТt matter,Т it said,) and then all the party were placed along the course, here and there. There was no СOne, two, three, and away,Т but they began running when they liked, and left off when they liked, so that it was not easy to know when the race was over. However, when they had been running half an hour or so, and were quite dry again, the Dodo suddenly called out СThe race is over!Т and they all crowded round it, panting, and asking, СBut who has won?Т

This question the Dodo could not answer without a great deal of thought, and it sat for a long time with one finger pressed upon its forehead (the position in which you usually see Shakespeare, in the pictures of him), while the rest waited in silence. At last the Dodo said, СEVERYBODY has won, and all must have prizes.Т

СBut who is to give the prizes?Т quite a chorus of voices asked.

СWhy, SHE, of course,Т said the Dodo, pointing to Alice with one finger; and the whole party at once crowded round her, calling out in a confused way, СPrizes! Prizes!Т

Alice had no idea what to do, and in despair she put her hand in her pocket, and pulled out a box of comfits[19 - Comfits Ц засахаренные орехи и сухофрукты.], (luckily the salt water had not got into it), and handed them round as prizes. There was exactly one apiece all round.

СBut she must have a prize herself, you know,Т said the Mouse.

СOf course,Т the Dodo replied very gravely. СWhat else have you got in your pocket?Т he went on, turning to Alice.

СOnly a thimble,Т said Alice sadly.

СHand it over here,Т said the Dodo.

Then they all crowded round her once more, while the Dodo solemnly presented the thimble, saying СWe beg your acceptance of this elegant thimbleТ; and, when it had finished this short speech, they all cheered.

Alice thought the whole thing very absurd, but they all looked so grave that she did not dare to laugh; and, as she could not think of anything to say, she simply bowed, and took the thimble, looking as solemn as she could.

The next thing was to eat the comfits: this caused some noise and confusion, as the large birds complained that they could not taste theirs, and the small ones choked and had to be patted on the back. However, it was over at last, and they sat down again in a ring, and begged the Mouse to tell them something more.

СYou are not attending!Т said the Mouse to Alice severely. СWhat are you thinking of?Т

СI beg your pardon,Т said Alice very humbly: Сyou had got to the fifth bend, I think?Т

СI shall do nothing of the sort,Т said the Mouse, getting up and walking away. СYou insult me by talking such nonsense!Т

СI didnТt mean it!Т pleaded poor Alice. СBut youТre so easily offended, you know!Т

The Mouse only growled in reply.

СPlease come back and finish your story!Т Alice called after it; and the others all joined in chorus, СYes, please do!Т but the Mouse only shook its head impatiently, and walked a little quicker.

СWhat a pity it wouldnТt stay!Т sighed the Lory, as soon as it was quite out of sight; and an old Crab took the opportunity of saying to her daughter СAh, my dear! Let this be a lesson to you never to lose YOUR temper!Т СHold your tongue, Ma!Т said the young Crab, a little snappishly. СYouТre enough to try the patience of an oyster!Т

СAnd who is Dinah, if I might venture to ask the question?Т said the Lory.

Alice replied eagerly, for she was always ready to talk about her pet: СDinahТs our cat. And sheТs such a capital one for catching mice you canТt think! And oh, I wish you could see her after the birds! Why, sheТll eat a little bird as soon as look at it!Т

This speech caused a remarkable sensation among the party. Some of the birds hurried off at once: one old Magpie began wrapping itself up very carefully, remarking, СI really must be getting home; the nightair doesnТt suit my throat!Т and a Canary called out in a trembling voice to its children, СCome away, my dears! ItТs high time you were all in bed!Т On various pretexts they all moved off, and Alice was soon left alone.

СI wish I hadnТt mentioned Dinah!Т she said to herself in a melancholy tone. СNobody seems to like her, down here, and IТm sure sheТs the best cat in the world! Oh, my dear Dinah! I wonder if I shall ever see you any more!Т And here poor Alice began to cry again, for she felt very lonely and lowspirited. In a little while, however, she again heard a little pattering of footsteps in the distance, and she looked up eagerly, half hoping that the Mouse had changed his mind, and was coming back to finish his story.




Chapter IV

The Rabbit Sends in a Little Bill


It was the White Rabbit, trotting slowly back again, and looking anxiously about as it went, as if it had lost something; and she heard it muttering to itself СThe Duchess! The Duchess! Oh my dear paws! Oh my fur and whiskers! SheТll get me executed, as sure as ferrets are ferrets! Where CAN I have dropped them, I wonder?Т Alice guessed in a moment that it was looking for the fan and the pair of white kid gloves, and she very goodnaturedly began hunting about for them, but they were nowhere to be seenЦeverything seemed to have changed since her swim in the pool, and the great hall, with the glass table and the little door, had vanished completely.

Very soon the Rabbit noticed Alice, as she went hunting about, and called out to her in an angry tone, СWhy, Mary Ann, what ARE you doing out here? Run home this moment, and fetch me a pair of gloves and a fan! Quick, now!Т And Alice was so much frightened that she ran off at once in the direction it pointed to, without trying to explain the mistake it had made.

СHe took me for his housemaid,Т she said to herself as she ran. СHow surprised heТll be when he finds out who I am! But IТd better take him his fan and glovesЦthat is, if I can find them.Т As she said this, she came upon a neat little house, on the door of which was a bright brass plate with the name СW. RABBITТ engraved upon it. She went in without knocking, and hurried upstairs, in great fear lest she should meet the real Mary Ann, and be turned out of the house before she had found the fan and gloves.

СHow queer it seems,Т Alice said to herself, Сto be going messages for a rabbit! I suppose DinahТll be sending me on messages next!Т And she began fancying the sort of thing that would happen: СУMiss Alice! Come here directly, and get ready for your walk!Ф УComing in a minute, nurse! But IТve got to see that the mouse doesnТt get out.Ф Only I donТt think,Т Alice went on, Сthat theyТd let Dinah stop in the house if it began ordering people about like that!Т

By this time she had found her way into a tidy little room with a table in the window, and on it (as she had hoped) a fan and two or three pairs of tiny white kid gloves: she took up the fan and a pair of the gloves, and was just going to leave the room, when her eye fell upon a little bottle that stood near the lookingglass. There was no label this time with the words СDRINK ME,Т but nevertheless she uncorked it and put it to her lips. СI know SOMETHING interesting is sure to happen,Т she said to herself, Сwhenever I eat or drink anything; so IТll just see what this bottle does. I do hope itТll make me grow large again, for really IТm quite tired of being such a tiny little thing!Т

It did so indeed, and much sooner than she had expected: before she had drunk half the bottle, she found her head pressing against the ceiling, and had to stoop to save her neck from being broken. She hastily put down the bottle, saying to herself СThatТs quite enough Ц I hope I shanТt grow any more Ц As it is, I canТt get out at the door Ц I do wish I hadnТt drunk quite so much!Т

Alas! it was too late to wish that! She went on growing, and growing, and very soon had to kneel down on the floor: in another minute there was not even room for this, and she tried the effect of lying down with one elbow against the door, and the other arm curled round her head. Still she went on growing, and, as a last resource, she put one arm out of the window, and one foot up the chimney, and said to herself СNow I can do no more, whatever happens. What WILL become of me?Т

Luckily for Alice, the little magic bottle had now had its full effect, and she grew no larger: still it was very uncomfortable, and, as there seemed to be no sort of chance of her ever getting out of the room again, no wonder she felt unhappy.

СIt was much pleasanter at home,Т thought poor Alice, Сwhen one wasnТt always growing larger and smaller, and being ordered about by mice and rabbits. I almost wish I hadnТt gone down that rabbithole Ц and yet Ц and yet Ц itТs rather curious, you know, this sort of life! I do wonder what CAN have happened to me! When I used to read fairytales, I fancied that kind of thing never happened, and now here I am in the middle of one! There ought to be a book written about me, that there ought! And when I grow up, IТll write one Ц but IТm grown up now,Т she added in a sorrowful tone; Сat least thereТs no room to grow up any more HERE.Т

СBut then,Т thought Alice, Сshall I NEVER get any older than I am now? ThatТll be a comfort, one way Ц never to be an old woman Ц but then Ц always to have lessons to learn! Oh, I shouldnТt like THAT!Т

СOh, you foolish Alice!Т she answered herself. СHow can you learn lessons in here? Why, thereТs hardly room for YOU, and no room at all for any lessonbooks!Т

And so she went on, taking first one side and then the other, and making quite a conversation of it altogether; but after a few minutes she heard a voice outside, and stopped to listen.

СMary Ann! Mary Ann!Т said the voice. СFetch me my gloves this moment!Т Then came a little pattering of feet on the stairs. Alice knew it was the Rabbit coming to look for her, and she trembled till she shook the house, quite forgetting that she was now about a thousand times as large as the Rabbit, and had no reason to be afraid of it.

Presently the Rabbit came up to the door, and tried to open it; but, as the door opened inwards, and AliceТs elbow was pressed hard against it, that attempt proved a failure. Alice heard it say to itself СThen IТll go round and get in at the window.Т

СTHAT you wonТtТ thought Alice, and, after waiting till she fancied she heard the Rabbit just under the window, she suddenly spread out her hand, and made a snatch in the air. She did not get hold of anything, but she heard a little shriek and a fall, and a crash of broken glass, from which she concluded that it was just possible it had fallen into a cucumberframe, or something of the sort.

Next came an angry voice Ц the RabbitТs Ц СPat! Pat! Where are you?Т And then a voice she had never heard before, СSure then IТm here! Digging for apples,[20 - Digging for apples Ц Pat Ц сокращение от Patrick, а ѕатрик считаетс€ самым распространенным ирландским именем. Ђ»рландские €блокиї Ц неофициальное название картошки.] yer honour!Т

СDigging for apples, indeed!Т said the Rabbit angrily. СHere! Come and help me out of THIS!Т (Sounds of more broken glass.)

СAn arm, you goose! Who ever saw one that size? Why, it fills the whole window!Т

СSure, it does, yer honour: but itТs an arm for all that.Т

There was a long silence after this, and Alice could only hear whispers now and then; such as, СSure, I donТt like it, yer honour, at all, at all!Т СDo as I tell you, you coward!Т and at last she spread out her hand again, and made another snatch in the air. This time there were TWO little shrieks, and more sounds of broken glass. СWhat a number of cucumberframes there must be!Т thought Alice. СI wonder what theyТll do next! As for pulling me out of the window, I only wish they COULD! IТm sure I donТt want to stay in here any longer!Т

She waited for some time without hearing anything more: at last came a rumbling of little cartwheels, and the sound of a good many voices all talking together: she made out the words: СWhereТs the other ladder? Ц Why, I hadnТt to bring but one; BillТs got the other Ц Bill! fetch it here, lad! Ц Here, put Сem up at this corner Ц No, tie Сem together first Ц they donТt reach half high enough yet Ц Oh! theyТll do well enough; donТt be particular Ц Here, Bill! catch hold of this ropeЦWill the roof bear? СOh! So BillТs got to come down the chimney, has he?Т said Alice to herself. СShy, they seem to put everything upon Bill! I wouldnТt be in BillТs place for a good deal: this fireplace is narrow, to be sure; but I THINK I can kick a little!Т

She drew her foot as far down the chimney as she could, and waited till she heard a little animal (she couldnТt guess of what sort it was) scratching and scrambling about in the chimney close above her: then, saying to herself СThis is Bill,Т she gave one sharp kick, and waited to see what would happen next.

The first thing she heard was a general chorus of СThere goes Bill!Т then the RabbitТs voice along Ц СCatch him, you by the hedge!Т then silence, and then another confusion of voices Ц СHold up his head Ц Brandy now ЦDonТt choke him Ц How was it, old fellow? Last came a little feeble, squeaking voice, (СThatТs Bill,Т thought Alice,) СWell, I hardly know Ц No more, thank ye; IТm better now Ц but IТm a deal too flustered to tell you Ц all I know is, something comes at me like a Jackinthebox, and up I goes like a skyrocket!Т

СSo you did, old fellow!Т said the others.

СWe must burn the house down!Т said the RabbitТs voice; and Alice called out as loud as she could, СIf you do. IТll set Dinah at you!Т

There was a dead silence instantly, and Alice thought to herself, СI wonder what they WILL do next! If they had any sense, theyТd take the roof off.Т After a minute or two, they began moving about again, and Alice heard the Rabbit say, СA barrowful will do, to begin with.Т

СA barrowful of WHAT?Т thought Alice; but she had not long to doubt, for the next moment a shower of little pebbles came rattling in at the window, and some of them hit her in the face. СIТll put a stop to this,Т she said to herself, and shouted out, СYouТd better not do that again!Т which produced another dead silence.

Alice noticed with some surprise that the pebbles were all turning into little cakes as they lay on the floor, and a bright idea came into her head. СIf I eat one of these cakes,Т she thought, СitТs sure to make SOME change in my size; and as it canТt possibly make me larger, it must make me smaller, I suppose.Т

So she swallowed one of the cakes, and was delighted to find that she began shrinking directly. As soon as she was small enough to get through the door, she ran out of the house, and found quite a crowd of little animals and birds waiting outside. The poor little Lizard, Bill, was in the middle, being held up by two guineapigs, who were giving it something out of a bottle. They all made a rush at Alice the moment she appeared; but she ran off as hard as she could, and soon found herself safe in a thick wood.

СThe first thing IТve got to do,Т said Alice to herself, as she wandered about in the wood, Сis to grow to my right size again; and the second thing is to find my way into that lovely garden. I think that will be the best plan.Т

It sounded an excellent plan, no doubt, and very neatly and simply arranged; the only difficulty was, that she had not the smallest idea how to set about it; and while she was peering about anxiously among the trees, a little sharp bark just over her head made her look up in a great hurry.

An enormous puppy was looking down at her with large round eyes, and feebly stretching out one paw, trying to touch her. СPoor little thing!Т said Alice, in a coaxing tone, and she tried hard to whistle to it; but she was terribly frightened all the time at the thought that it might be hungry, in which case it would be very likely to eat her up in spite of all her coaxing.

Hardly knowing what she did, she picked up a little bit of stick, and held it out to the puppy; whereupon the puppy jumped into the air off all its feet at once, with a yelp of delight, and rushed at the stick, and made believe to worry it; then Alice dodged behind a great thistle, to keep herself from being run over; and the moment she appeared on the other side, the puppy made another rush at the stick, and tumbled head over heels in its hurry to get hold of it; then Alice, thinking it was very like having a game of play with a carthorse, and expecting every moment to be trampled under its feet, ran round the thistle again; then the puppy began a series of short charges at the stick, running a very little way forwards each time and a long way back, and barking hoarsely all the while, till at last it sat down a good way off, panting, with its tongue hanging out of its mouth, and its great eyes half shut.

This seemed to Alice a good opportunity for making her escape; so she set off at once, and ran till she was quite tired and out of breath, and till the puppyТs bark sounded quite faint in the distance.

СAnd yet what a dear little puppy it was!Т said Alice, as she leant against a buttercup to rest herself, and fanned herself with one of the leaves: СI should have liked teaching it tricks very much, if Ц if IТd only been the right size to do it! Oh dear! IТd nearly forgotten that IТve got to grow up again! Let me see Ц how IS it to be managed? I suppose I ought to eat or drink something or other; but the great question is, what?Т

The great question certainly was, what? Alice looked all round her at the flowers and the blades of grass, but she did not see anything that looked like the right thing to eat or drink under the circumstances. There was a large mushroom growing near her, about the same height as herself; and when she had looked under it, and on both sides of it, and behind it, it occurred to her that she might as well look and see what was on the top of it.

She stretched herself up on tiptoe, and peeped over the edge of the mushroom, and her eyes immediately met those of a large caterpillar, that was sitting on the top with its arms folded, quietly smoking a long hookah, and taking not the smallest notice of her or of anything else.




Chapter V

Advice from a Caterpillar


The Caterpillar and Alice looked at each other for some time in silence: at last the Caterpillar took the hookah out of its mouth, and addressed her in a languid, sleepy voice.

СWho are YOU?Т said the Caterpillar.

This was not an encouraging opening for a conversation. Alice replied, rather shyly, СI Ц I hardly know, sir, just at present Ц at least I know who I WAS when I got up this morning, but I think I must have been changed several times since then.Т

СWhat do you mean by that?Т said the Caterpillar sternly. СExplain yourself!Т

СI canТt explain MYSELF, IТm afraid, sirТ said Alice, Сbecause IТm not myself, you see.Т

СI donТt see,Т said the Caterpillar.

СIТm afraid I canТt put it more clearly,Т Alice replied very politely, Сfor I canТt understand it myself to begin with; and being so many different sizes in a day is very confusing.Т

СIt isnТt,Т said the Caterpillar.

СWell, perhaps you havenТt found it so yet,Т said Alice; Сbut when you have to turn into a chrysalis Ц you will some day, you know Ц and then after that into a butterfly, I should think youТll feel it a little queer, wonТt you?Т

СNot a bit,Т said the Caterpillar.

СWell, perhaps your feelings may be different,Т said Alice; Сall I know is, it would feel very queer to ME.Т

СYou!Т said the Caterpillar contemptuously. СWho are YOU?Т

Which brought them back again to the beginning of the conversation. Alice felt a little irritated at the CaterpillarТs making such VERY short remarks, and she drew herself up and said, very gravely, СI think, you ought to tell me who YOU are, first.Т

СWhy?Т said the Caterpillar.

Here was another puzzling question; and as Alice could not think of any good reason, and as the Caterpillar seemed to be in a VERY unpleasant state of mind, she turned away.

СCome back!Т the Caterpillar called after her. СIТve something important to say!Т

This sounded promising, certainly: Alice turned and came back again.

СKeep your temper,Т said the Caterpillar.

СIs that all?Т said Alice, swallowing down her anger as well as she could.

СNo,Т said the Caterpillar.

Alice thought she might as well wait, as she had nothing else to do, and perhaps after all it might tell her something worth hearing. For some minutes it puffed away without speaking, but at last it unfolded its arms, took the hookah out of its mouth again, and said, СSo you think youТre changed, do you?Т

СIТm afraid I am, sir,Т said Alice; СI canТt remember things as I used Ц and I donТt keep the same size for ten minutes together!Т

СCanТt remember WHAT things?Т said the Caterpillar.

СWell, IТve tried to say СHOW DOTH THE LITTLE BUSY BEE,Т but it all came different!Т Alice replied in a very melancholy voice.

СRepeat УYOU ARE OLD, FATHER WILLIAM,Ф[21 - УYouТre old, Father WilliamФ Ц это перва€ строчка попул€рного детского стихотворени€ –оберта —аути (1744Ц1832), Ђ”тешени€ старика и как он их получилї.]Т said the Caterpillar.

Alice folded her hands, and began: Ц

		СYou are old, Father William,Т the young man said,
		СAnd your hair has become very white;
		And yet you incessantly stand on your head Ц
		Do you think, at your age, it is right?Т

		СIn my youth,Т Father William replied to his son,
		СI feared it might injure the brain;
		But, now that IТm perfectly sure I have none,
		Why, I do it again and again.Т

		СYou are old,Т said the youth, Сas I mentioned before,
		And have grown most uncommonly fat;
		Yet you turned a backsomersault in at the door Ц
		Pray, what is the reason of that?Т

		СI kept all my limbs very supple

СIt is wrong from beginning to end,Т said the Caterpillar decidedly, and there was silence for some minutes.

The Caterpillar was the first to speak.

СWhat size do you want to be?Т it asked.

СOh, IТm not particular as to size,Т Alice hastily replied; Сonly one doesnТt like changing so often, you know.Т

Alice said nothing: she had never been so much contradicted in her life before, and she felt that she was losing her temper.

СAre you content now?Т said the Caterpillar.

СWell, I should like to be a LITTLE larger, sir, if you wouldnТt mind,Т said Alice: Сthree inches is such a wretched height to be.Т

СIt is a very good height indeed!Т said the Caterpillar angrily, rearing itself upright as it spoke (it was exactly three inches high).

СBut IТm not used to it!Т pleaded poor Alice in a piteous tone. And she thought of herself, СI wish the creatures wouldnТt be so easily offended!Т

СYouТll get used to it in time,Т said the Caterpillar; and it put the hookah into its mouth and began smoking again.

This time Alice waited patiently until it chose to speak again. In a minute or two the Caterpillar took the hookah out of its mouth and yawned once or twice, and shook itself. Then it got down off the mushroom, and crawled away in the grass, merely remarking as it went, СOne side will make you grow taller, and the other side will make you grow shorter.Т

СOne side of WHAT? The other side of WHAT?Т thought Alice to herself.

СOf the mushroom,Т said the Caterpillar, just as if she had asked it aloud; and in another moment it was out of sight.

Alice remained looking thoughtfully at the mushroom for a minute, trying to make out which were the two sides of it; and as it was perfectly round, she found this a very difficult question. However, at last she stretched her arms round it as far as they would go, and broke off a bit of the edge with each hand.

СAnd now which is which?Т she said to herself, and nibbled a little of the righthand bit to try the effect: the next moment she felt a violent blow underneath her chin: it had struck her foot!

She was a good deal frightened by this very sudden change, but she felt that there was no time to be lost, as she was shrinking rapidly; so she set to work at once to eat some of the other bit. Her chin was pressed so closely against her foot, that there was hardly room to open her mouth; but she did it at last, and managed to swallow a morsel of the lefthand bit.


* * *

СCome, my headТs free at last!Т said Alice in a tone of delight, which changed into alarm in another moment, when she found that her shoulders were nowhere to be found: all she could see, when she looked down, was an immense length of neck, which seemed to rise like a stalk out of a sea of green leaves that lay far below her.

СWhat CAN all that green stuff be?Т said Alice. СAnd where HAVE my shoulders got to? And oh, my poor hands, how is it I canТt see you?Т She was moving them about as she spoke, but no result seemed to follow, except a little shaking among the distant green leaves.

As there seemed to be no chance of getting her hands up to her head, she tried to get her head down to them, and was delighted to find that her neck would bend about easily in any direction, like a serpent. She had just succeeded in curving it down into a graceful zigzag, and was going to dive in among the leaves, which she found to be nothing but the tops of the trees under which she had been wandering, when a sharp hiss made her draw back in a hurry: a large pigeon had flown into her face, and was beating her violently with its wings.

СSerpent!Т screamed the Pigeon.

СIТm NOT a serpent!Т said Alice indignantly. СLet me alone!Т

СSerpent, I say again!Т repeated the Pigeon, but in a more subdued tone, and added with a kind of sob, СIТve tried every way, and nothing seems to suit them!Т

СI havenТt the least idea what youТre talking about,Т said Alice.

СIТve tried the roots of trees, and IТve tried banks, and IТve tried hedges,Т the Pigeon went on, without attending to her; Сbut those serpents! ThereТs no pleasing them!Т

Alice was more and more puzzled, but she thought there was no use in saying anything more till the Pigeon had finished.

СAs if it wasnТt trouble enough hatching the eggs,Т said the Pigeon; Сbut I must be on the lookout for serpents night and day! Why, I havenТt had a wink of sleep these three weeks!Т

СIТm very sorry youТve been annoyed,Т said Alice, who was beginning to see its meaning.

СAnd just as IТd taken the highest tree in the wood,Т continued the Pigeon, raising its voice to a shriek, Сand just as I was thinking I should be free of them at last, they must needs come wriggling down from the sky! Ugh, Serpent!Т

СBut IТm NOT a serpent, I tell you!Т said Alice. СIТm a Ц IТm aЦТ

СWell! WHAT are you?Т said the Pigeon. СI can see youТre trying to invent something!Т

СI Ц IТm a little girl,Т said Alice, rather doubtfully, as she remembered the number of changes she had gone through that day.

СA likely story indeed!Т said the Pigeon in a tone of the deepest contempt. СIТve seen a good many little girls in my time, but never ONE with such a neck as that! No, no! YouТre a serpent; and thereТs no use denying it. I suppose youТll be telling me next that you never tasted an egg!Т

СI HAVE tasted eggs, certainly,Т said Alice, who was a very truthful child; Сbut little girls eat eggs quite as much as serpents do, you know.Т

СI donТt believe it,Т said the Pigeon; Сbut if they do, why then theyТre a kind of serpent, thatТs all I can say.Т

This was such a new idea to Alice, that she was quite silent for a minute or two, which gave the Pigeon the opportunity of adding, СYouТre looking for eggs, I know THAT well enough; and what does it matter to me whether youТre a little girl or a serpent?Т

СIt matters a good deal to ME,Т said Alice hastily; Сbut IТm not looking for eggs, as it happens; and if I was, I shouldnТt want YOURS: I donТt like them raw.Т

СWell, be off, then!Т said the Pigeon in a sulky tone, as it settled down again into its nest. Alice crouched down among the trees as well as she could, for her neck kept getting entangled among the branches, and every now and then she had to stop and untwist it. After a while she remembered that she still held the pieces of mushroom in her hands, and she set to work very carefully, nibbling first at one and then at the other, and growing sometimes taller and sometimes shorter, until she had succeeded in bringing herself down to her usual height.

It was so long since she had been anything near the right size, that it felt quite strange at first; but she got used to it in a few minutes, and began talking to herself, as usual. СCome, thereТs half my plan done now! How puzzling all these changes are! IТm never sure what IТm going to be, from one minute to another! However, IТve got back to my right size: the next thing is, to get into that beautiful garden Ц how IS that to be done, I wonder?Т As she said this, she came suddenly upon an open place, with a little house in it about four feet high. СWhoever lives there,Т thought Alice, СitТll never do to come upon them THIS size: why, I should frighten them out of their wits!Т So she began nibbling at the righthand bit again, and did not venture to go near the house till she had brought herself down to nine inches high.




Chapter VI

Pig and Pepper


For a minute or two she stood looking at the house, and wondering what to do next, when suddenly a footman in livery came running out of the wood Ц (she considered him to be a footman because he was in livery: otherwise, judging by his face only, she would have called him a fish) Ц and rapped loudly at the door with his knuckles. It was opened by another footman in livery, with a round face, and large eyes like a frog; and both footmen, Alice noticed, had powdered hair that curled all over their heads. She felt very curious to know what it was all about, and crept a little way out of the wood to listen.

The FishFootman began by producing from under his arm a great letter, nearly as large as himself, and this he handed over to the other, saying, in a solemn tone, СFor the Duchess. An invitation from the Queen to play croquet[22 - Croquet Ц крокет, игра, в которую играют м€чами и молотками с длинными руко€тками. ћ€чи нужно направл€ть сквозь воротца/обручи/калитки.  рокет был попул€рен во второй половине XIX в., особенно в ¬икторианскую эпоху.].Т The FrogFootman repeated, in the same solemn tone, only changing the order of the words a little, СFrom the Queen. An invitation for the Duchess to play croquet.Т

Then they both bowed low, and their curls got entangled together.

Alice laughed so much at this, that she had to run back into the wood for fear of their hearing her; and when she next peeped out the FishFootman was gone, and the other was sitting on the ground near the door, staring stupidly up into the sky.

Alice went timidly up to the door, and knocked.

ThereТs no sort of use in knocking,Т said the Footman, Сand that for two reasons. СPlease, then,Т said Alice, Сhow am I to get in?Т

СThere might be some sense in your knocking,Т the Footman went on without attending to her, Сif we had the door between us. For instance, if you were INSIDE, you might knock, and I could let you out, you know.Т He was looking up into the sky all the time he was speaking, and this Alice thought decidedly uncivil. СBut perhaps he canТt help it,Т she said to herself; Сhis eyes are so VERY nearly at the top of his head. But at any rate he might answer questions. Ц How am I to get in?Т she repeated, aloud.

СI shall sit here,Т the Footman remarked, Сtill tomorrowЦТ

At this moment the door of the house opened, and a large plate came skimming out, straight at the FootmanТs head: it just grazed his nose, and broke to pieces against one of the trees behind him.

СЦor next day, maybe,Т the Footman continued in the same tone, exactly as if nothing had happened.

СHow am I to get in?Т asked Alice again, in a louder tone.

СARE you to get in at all?Т said the Footman. СThatТs the first question, you know.Т

It was, no doubt: only Alice did not like to be told so. СItТs really dreadful,Т she muttered to herself, Сthe way all the creatures argue. ItТs enough to drive one crazy!Т

The Footman seemed to think this a good opportunity for repeating his remark, with variations. СI shall sit here,Т he said, Сon and off, for days and days.Т

СBut what am I to do?Т said Alice.

СAnything you like,Т said the Footman, and began whistling.

СOh, thereТs no use in talking to him,Т said Alice desperately: СheТs perfectly idiotic!Т And she opened the door and went in.

The door led right into a large kitchen, which was full of smoke from one end to the other: the Duchess was sitting on a threelegged stool in the middle, nursing a baby; the cook was leaning over the fire, stirring a large cauldron which seemed to be full of soup.

СThereТs certainly too much pepper in that soup!Т Alice said to herself, as well as she could for sneezing.

There was certainly too much of it in the air. Even the Duchess sneezed occasionally; and as for the baby, it was sneezing and howling alternately without a momentТs pause. The only things in the kitchen that did not sneeze, were the cook, and a large cat which was sitting on the hearth and grinning from ear to ear.

СPlease would you tell me,Т said Alice, a little timidly, for she was not quite sure whether it was good manners for her to speak first, Сwhy your cat grins like that?Т

СItТs a Cheshire cat[23 - Cheshire cat Ц чеширский кот. ќбраз наве€н поговоркой Ђухмыл€етс€, как чеширский котї.  роме того, утверждают, что Ћьюис  эрролл видел изображение улыбающегос€ кота в церкви  рофтон“ис, где его отец служил ректором],Т said the Duchess, Сand thatТs why. Pig!Т

		СI speak severely to my boy,
		$$$I beat him when he sneezes;
		For he can thoroughly enjoy
		$$$The pepper when he pleases!Т

		CHORUS.

		СWow! wow! wow!Т

СHere! you may nurse it a bit, if you like!Т the Duchess said to Alice, flinging the baby at her as she spoke. СI must go and get ready to play croquet with the Queen,Т and she hurried out of the room. The cook threw a fryingpan after her as she went out, but it just missed her.

Alice caught the baby with some difficulty, as it was a queershaped little creature, and held out its arms and legs in all directions, Сjust like a starfish,Т thought Alice. The poor little thing was snorting like a steamengine when she caught it, and kept doubling itself up and straightening itself out again, so that altogether, for the first minute or two, it was as much as she could do to hold it.

As soon as she had made out the proper way of nursing it, (which was to twist it up into a sort of knot, and then keep tight hold of its right ear and left foot, so as to prevent its undoing itself,) she carried it out into the open air. СIF I donТt take this child away with me,Т thought Alice, СtheyТre sure to kill it in a day or two: wouldnТt it be murder to leave it behind?Т She said the last words out loud, and the little thing grunted in reply (it had left off sneezing by this time). СDonТt grunt,Т said Alice; СthatТs not at all a proper way of expressing yourself.Т

The baby grunted again, and Alice looked very anxiously into its face to see what was the matter with it. There could be no doubt that it had a VERY turnup nose, much more like a snout than a real nose; also its eyes were getting extremely small for a baby: altogether Alice did not like the look of the thing at all. СBut perhaps it was only sobbing,Т she thought, and looked into its eyes again, to see if there were any tears.

No, there were no tears. СIf youТre going to turn into a pig, my dear,Т said Alice, seriously, СIТll have nothing more to do with you. Mind now!Т The poor little thing sobbed again (or grunted, it was impossible to say which), and they went on for some while in silence.

Alice was just beginning to think to herself, СNow, what am I to do with this creature when I get it home?Т when it grunted again, so violently, that she looked down into its face in some alarm. This time there could be NO mistake about it: it was neither more nor less than a pig, and she felt that it would be quite absurd for her to carry it further.

So she set the little creature down, and felt quite relieved to see it trot away quietly into the wood. СIf it had grown up,Т she said to herself, Сit would have made a dreadfully ugly child: but it makes rather a handsome pig, I think.Т And she began thinking over other children she knew, who might do very well as pigs, and was just saying to herself, Сif one only knew the right way to change themЦТ when she was a little startled by seeing the Cheshire Cat sitting on a bough of a tree a few yards[26 - Yards Ц €рды, британские единицы измерени€ рассто€ни€. 1 €рд = 0,91 см.] off.

The Cat only grinned when it saw Alice. It looked goodnatured, she thought: still it had VERY long claws and a great many teeth, so she felt that it ought to be treated with respect.

СCheshire Puss,Т she began, rather timidly, as she did not at all know whether it would like the name: however, it only grinned a little wider. СCome, itТs pleased so far,Т thought Alice, and she went on. СWould you tell me, please, which way I ought to go from here?Т

СThat depends a good deal on where you want to get to,Т said the Cat.

СWell, then,Т the Cat went on, Сyou see, a dog growls when itТs angry, and wags its tail when itТs pleased. Now I growl when IТm pleased, and wag my tail when IТm angry. Therefore IТm mad.Т

СI call it purring, not growling,Т said Alice.

СI should like it very much,Т said Alice, Сbut I havenТt been invited yet.Т

СYouТll see me there,Т said the Cat, and vanished.

Alice was not much surprised at this, she was getting so used to queer things happening. While she was looking at the place where it had been, it suddenly appeared again.

СBythebye, what became of the baby?Т said the Cat. СIТd nearly forgotten to ask.Т

СIt turned into a pig,Т Alice quietly said, just as if it had come back in a natural way.

СI thought it would,Т said the Cat, and vanished again.

Alice waited a little, half expecting to see it again, but it did not appear, and after a minute or two she walked on in the direction in which the March Hare was said to live. СIТve seen hatters before,Т she said to herself; Сthe March Hare will be much the most interesting, and perhaps as this is May it wonТt be raving madЦat least not so mad as it was in March.Т As she said this, she looked up, and there was the Cat again, sitting on a branch of a tree.

СDid you say pig, or fig?Т said the Cat.

СI said pig,Т replied Alice; Сand I wish you wouldnТt keep appearing and vanishing so suddenly: you make one quite giddy.Т

СAll right,Т said the Cat; and this time it vanished quite slowly, beginning with the end of the tail, and ending with the grin, which remained some time after the rest of it had gone.

СWell! IТve often seen a cat without a grin,Т thought Alice; Сbut a grin without a cat! ItТs the most curious thing I ever saw in my life!Т

She had not gone much farther before she came in sight of the house of the March Hare: she thought it must be the right house, because the chimneys were shaped like ears and the roof was thatched with fur. It was so large a house, that she did not like to go nearer till she had nibbled some more of the lefthand bit of mushroom, and raised herself to about two feet high: even then she walked up towards it rather timidly, saying to herself СSuppose it should be raving mad after all! I almost wish IТd gone to see the Hatter instead!Т




Chapter VII

A Mad Tea-Party


There was a table set out under a tree in front of the house, and the March Hare and the Hatter were having tea at it: a Dormouse was sitting between them, fast asleep, and the other two were using it as a cushion, resting their elbows on it, and talking over its head. СVery uncomfortable for the Dormouse,Т thought Alice; Сonly, as itТs asleep, I suppose it doesnТt mind.Т

The table was a large one, but the three were all crowded together at one corner of it: СNo room! No room!Т they cried out when they saw Alice coming. СThereТs PLENTY of room!Т said Alice indignantly, and she sat down in a large armchair at one end of the table.

СHave some wine,Т the March Hare said in an encouraging tone.

Alice looked all round the table, but there was nothing on it but tea. СI donТt see any wine,Т she remarked.

СThere isnТt any,Т said the March Hare.

СThen it wasnТt very civil of you to offer it,Т said Alice angrily.

СIt wasnТt very civil of you to sit down without being invited,Т said the March Hare.

СI didnТt know it was YOUR table,Т said Alice; СitТs laid for a great many more than three.Т

СYour hair wants cutting,Т said the Hatter. He had been looking at Alice for some time with great curiosity, and this was his first speech.

СYou should learn not to make personal remarks,Т Alice said with some severity; СitТs very rude.Т

The Hatter opened his eyes very wide on hearing this; but all he SAID was, СWhy is a raven like a writingdesk?Т

СCome, we shall have some fun now!Т thought Alice. СIТm glad theyТve begun asking riddles. Ц I believe I can guess that,Т she added aloud.

СDo you mean that you think you can find out the answer to it?Т said the March Hare.

СExactly so,Т said Alice.

СThen you should say what you mean,Т the March Hare went on.

СI do,Т Alice hastily replied; Сat least Ц at least I mean what I say Ц thatТs the same thing, you know.Т

СNot the same thing a bit!Т said the Hatter. СYou might just as well say that УI see what I eatФ is the same thing as УI eat what I seeФ!Т

СYou might just as well say,Т added the March Hare, Сthat УI like what I getФ is the same thing as УI get what I likeФ!Т

СYou might just as well say,Т added the Dormouse, who seemed to be talking in his sleep, Сthat УI breathe when I sleepФ is the same thing as УI sleep when I breatheФ!Т

СIt IS the same thing with you,Т said the Hatter, and here the conversation dropped, and the party sat silent for a minute, while Alice thought over all she could remember about ravens and writingdesks, which wasnТt much.

The Hatter was the first to break the silence. СWhat day of the month is it?Т he said, turning to Alice: he had taken his watch out of his pocket, and was looking at it uneasily, shaking it every now and then, and holding it to his ear.

Alice considered a little, and then said СThe fourth.[28 - The fourth. Ц 4 ма€ Ц день рождени€ јлисы Ћидделл. ћес€ц упоминалс€ в предыдущей главе.]Т

This piece of rudeness was more than Alice could bear: she got up in great disgust, and walked off; the Dormouse fell asleep instantly, and neither of the others took the least notice of her going, though she looked back once or twice, half hoping that they would call after her: the last time she saw them, they were trying to put the Dormouse into the teapot.

СAt any rate IТll never go THERE again!Т said Alice as she picked her way through the wood. СItТs the stupidest teaparty I ever was at in all my life!Т

Just as she said this, she noticed that one of the trees had a door leading right into it. СThatТs very curious!Т she thought. СBut everythingТs curious today. I think I may as well go in at once.Т And in she went.

Once more she found herself in the long hall, and close to the little glass table. СNow, IТll manage better this time,Т she said to herself, and began by taking the little golden key, and unlocking the door that led into the garden. Then she went to work nibbling at the mushroom (she had kept a piece of it in her pocket) till she was about a foot high: then she walked down the little passage: and THEN Ц she found herself at last in the beautiful garden, among the bright flowerbeds and the cool fountains.




Chapter VIII

The QueenТs Croquet-Ground


A large rosetree stood near the entrance of the garden: the roses growing on it were white, but there were three gardeners at it, busily painting them red.[31 - Ёто вполне очевидна€ отсылка к войне јлой и белой розы, династической войне между Ћанкастерами и …орками, чьими символами были ала€ и бела€ роза соответственно.] Alice thought this a very curious thing, and she went nearer to watch them, and just as she came up to them she heard one of them say, СLook out now, Five! First came ten soldiers carrying clubs; these were all shaped like the three gardeners, oblong and flat, with their hands and feet at the corners: next the ten courtiers; these were ornamented all over with diamonds, and walked two and two, as the soldiers did. After these came the royal children; there were ten of them, and the little dears came jumping merrily along hand in hand, in couples: they were all ornamented with hearts. Next came the guests, mostly Kings and Queens, and among them Alice recognised the White Rabbit: it was talking in a hurried nervous manner, smiling at everything that was said, and went by without noticing her. Then followed the Knave of Hearts, carrying the KingТs crown on a crimson velvet cushion; and, last of all this grand procession, came THE KING AND QUEEN OF HEARTS.

Alice was rather doubtful whether she ought not to lie down on her face like the three gardeners, but she could not remember ever having heard of such a rule at processions; Сand besides, what would be the use of a procession,Т thought she, Сif people had all to lie down upon their faces, so that they couldnТt see it?Т So she stood still where she was, and waited.

When the procession came opposite to Alice, they all stopped and looked at her, and the Queen said severely СWho is this?Т She said it to the Knave of Hearts, who only bowed and smiled in reply.

СIdiot!Т said the Queen, tossing her head impatiently; and, turning to Alice, she went on, СWhatТs your name, child?Т

СGet to your places!Т shouted the Queen in a voice of thunder, and people began running about in all directions, tumbling up against each other; however, they got settled down in a minute or two, and the game began. Alice thought she had never seen such a curious croquetground in her life; it was all ridges and furrows; the balls were live hedgehogs, the mallets live flamingoes, and the soldiers had to double themselves up and to stand on their hands and feet, to make the arches.

The chief difficulty Alice found at first was in managing her flamingo: she succeeded in getting its body tucked away, comfortably enough, under her arm, with its legs hanging down, but generally, just as she had got its neck nicely straightened out, and was going to give the hedgehog a blow with its head, it WOULD twist itself round and look up in her face, with such a puzzled expression that she could not help bursting out laughing: and when she had got its head down, and was going to begin again, it was very provoking to find that the hedgehog had unrolled itself, and was in the act of crawling away: besides all this, there was generally a ridge or furrow in the way wherever she wanted to send the hedgehog to, and, as the doubledup soldiers were always getting up and walking off to other parts of the ground, Alice soon came to the conclusion that it was a very difficult game indeed.

The players all played at once without waiting for turns, quarrelling all the while, and fighting for the hedgehogs; and in a very short time the Queen was in a furious passion, and went stamping about, and shouting СOff with his head!Т or СOff with her head!Т about once in a minute.

Alice began to feel very uneasy: to be sure, she had not as yet had any dispute with the Queen, but she knew that it might happen any minute, Сand then,Т thought she, Сwhat would become of me? TheyТre dreadfully fond of beheading people here; the great wonder is, that thereТs any one left alive!Т

She was looking about for some way of escape, and wondering whether she could get away without being seen, when she noticed a curious appearance in the air: it puzzled her very much at first, but, after watching it a minute or two, she made it out to be a grin, and she said to herself СItТs the Cheshire Cat: now I shall have somebody to talk to.Т

СHow are you getting on?Т said the Cat, as soon as there was mouth enough for it to speak with.

Alice waited till the eyes appeared, and then nodded. СI donТt think they play at all fairly,Т Alice began, in rather a complaining tone, Сand they all quarrel so dreadfully one canТt hear oneself speak Ц and they donТt seem to have any rules in particular; at least, if there are, nobody attends to them Ц and youТve no idea how confusing it is all the things being alive; for instance, thereТs the arch IТve got to go through next walking about at the other end of the ground Ц and I should have croqueted the QueenТs hedgehog just now, only it ran away when it saw mine coming!Т

СHow do you like the Queen?Т said the Cat in a low voice.

СNot at all,Т said Alice: СsheТs so extremelyЦТ Just then she noticed that the Queen was close behind her, listening: so she went on, СЦlikely to win, that itТs hardly worth while finishing the game.Т

The Queen smiled and passed on.

СWho ARE you talking to?Т said the King, going up to Alice, and looking at the CatТs head with great curiosity.

СItТs a friend of mine Ц a Cheshire Cat,Т said Alice: Сallow me to introduce it.Т

СI donТt like the look of it at all,Т said the King: Сhowever, it may kiss my hand if it likes.Т

СIТd rather not,Т the Cat remarked.

СDonТt be impertinent,Т said the King, Сand donТt look at me like that!Т He got behind Alice as he spoke.

СA cat may look at a king,Т[32 - A cat may look at a king Ц Ёта пословица означает, что, какой бы важной персоной человек ни был, на него все равно могут и будут смотреть те, чей статус ниже. »спользование пословиц и устойчивых выражений в буквальном смысле дл€ описани€ ситуации Ц один из характерных приемов британского юмора.] said Alice.
