СWell, it must be removed,Т said the King very decidedly, and he called the Queen, who was passing at the moment, СMy dear! I wish you would have this cat removed!Т

The Queen had only one way of settling all difficulties, great or small. СOff with his head!Т she said, without even looking round.

СIТll fetch the executioner myself,Т said the King eagerly, and he hurried off.

Alice thought she might as well go back, and see how the game was going on, as she heard the QueenТs voice in the distance, screaming with passion. She had already heard her sentence three of the players to be executed for having missed their turns, and she did not like the look of things at all, as the game was in such confusion that she never knew whether it was her turn or not. So she went in search of her hedgehog.

The hedgehog was engaged in a fight with another hedgehog, which seemed to Alice an excellent opportunity for croqueting one of them with the other: the only difficulty was, that her flamingo was gone across to the other side of the garden, where Alice could see it trying in a helpless sort of way to fly up into a tree.

By the time she had caught the flamingo and brought it back, the fight was over, and both the hedgehogs were out of sight: Сbut it doesnТt matter much,Т thought Alice, Сas all the arches are gone from this side of the ground.Т So she tucked it away under her arm, that it might not escape again, and went back for a little more conversation with her friend.

When she got back to the Cheshire Cat, she was surprised to find quite a large crowd collected round it: there was a dispute going on between the executioner, the King, and the Queen, who were all talking at once, while all the rest were quite silent, and looked very uncomfortable.

The moment Alice appeared, she was appealed to by all three to settle the question, and they repeated their arguments to her, though, as they all spoke at once, she found it very hard indeed to make out exactly what they said.

The executionerТs argument was, that you couldnТt cut off a head unless there was a body to cut it off from: that he had never had to do such a thing before, and he wasnТt going to begin at HIS time of life.

The KingТs argument was, that anything that had a head could be beheaded, and that you werenТt to talk nonsense.

The QueenТs argument was, that if something wasnТt done about it in less than no time sheТd have everybody executed, all round. (It was this last remark that had made the whole party look so grave and anxious.)

Alice could think of nothing else to say but СIt belongs to the Duchess: youТd better ask HER about it.Т

СSheТs in prison,Т the Queen said to the executioner: Сfetch her here.Т And the executioner went off like an arrow.

The CatТs head began fading away the moment he was gone, and, by the time he had come back with the Dutchess, it had entirely disappeared; so the King and the executioner ran wildly up and down looking for it, while the rest of the party went back to the game.




Chapter IX

The Mock TurtleТs Story


СYou canТt think how glad I am to see you again, you dear old thing!Т said the Duchess, as she tucked her arm affectionately into AliceТs, and they walked off together.

СIТve a right to think,Т said Alice sharply, for she was beginning to feel a little worried.

СJust about as much right,Т said the Duchess, Сas pigs have to fly; and the mЦТ

But here, to AliceТs great surprise, the DuchessТs voice died away, even in the middle of her favourite word Сmoral,Т and the arm that was linked into hers began to tremble. Alice looked up, and there stood the Queen in front of them, with her arms folded, frowning like a thunderstorm.

СA fine day, your Majesty!Т the Duchess began in a low, weak voice.

СNow, I give you fair warning,Т shouted the Queen, stamping on the ground as she spoke; Сeither you or your head must be off, and that in about half no time! Take your choice!Т

The Duchess took her choice, and was gone in a moment.

СLetТs go on with the game,Т the Queen said to Alice; and Alice was too much frightened to say a word, but slowly followed her back to the croquetground.

The other guests had taken advantage of the QueenТs absence, and were resting in the shade: however, the moment they saw her, they hurried back to the game, the Queen merely remarking that a momentТs delay would cost them their lives.

All the time they were playing the Queen never left off quarrelling with the other players, and shouting СOff with his head!Т or СOff with her head!Т Those whom she sentenced were taken into custody by the soldiers, who of course had to leave off being arches to do this, so that by the end of half an hour or so there were no arches left, and all the players, except the King, the Queen, and Alice, were in custody and under sentence of execution.

Then the Queen left off, quite out of breath, and said to Alice, СHave you seen the Mock Turtle yet?Т

СNo,Т said Alice. СI donТt even know what a Mock Turtle is.Т

СItТs the thing Mock Turtle Soup is made from,Т[34 - Ђ‘альшивый черепаховый супї в действительности варили из тел€чьих головы, ножек с копытами и хвоста. ќн готовилс€ на гов€жьем бульоне с мадерой. ѕоэтому у нарисованного ƒжоном “енниелом персонажа тел€чь€ голова, копыта и хвост.] said the Queen.

СI never saw one, or heard of one,Т said Alice.

СCome on, then,Т said the Queen, Сand he shall tell you his history,Т

As they walked off together, Alice heard the King say in a low voice, to the company generally, СYou are all pardoned.Т СCome, THATТS a good thing!Т she said to herself, for she had felt quite unhappy at the number of executions the Queen had ordered.

They very soon came upon a Gryphon, lying fast asleep in the sun. (IF you donТt know what a Gryphon is, look at the picture.) Alice did not quite like the look of the creature, but on the whole she thought it would be quite as safe to stay with it as to go after that savage Queen: so she waited.

The Gryphon sat up and rubbed its eyes: then it watched the Queen till she was out of sight: then it chuckled. СWhat fun!Т said the Gryphon, half to itself, half to Alice.

СWhat IS the fun?Т said Alice.

They had not gone far before they saw the Mock Turtle in the distance, sitting sad and lonely on a little ledge of rock, and, as they came nearer, Alice could hear him sighing as if his heart would break. She pitied him deeply. СWhat is his sorrow?Т she asked the Gryphon, and the Gryphon answered, very nearly in the same words as before, СItТs all his fancy, that: he hasnТt got no sorrow, you know. So they went up to the Mock Turtle, who looked at them with large eyes full of tears, but said nothing.

Chapter XI

Who Stole the Tarts?


The King and Queen of Hearts were seated on their throne when they arrived, with a great crowd assembled about them Ц all sorts of little birds and beasts, as well as the whole pack of cards: the Knave was standing before them, in chains, with a soldier on each side to guard him; and near the King was the White Rabbit, with a trumpet in one hand, and a scroll of parchment in the other. In the very middle of the court was a table, with a large dish of tarts upon it: they looked so good, that it made Alice quite hungry to look at them Ц СI wish theyТd get the trial done,Т she thought, Сand hand round the refreshments!Т But there seemed to be no chance of this, so she began looking at everything about her, to pass away the time.

Alice had never been in a court of justice before, but she had read about them in books, and she was quite pleased to find that she knew the name of nearly everything there. СThatТs the judge,Т she said to herself, Сbecause of his great wig.Т

The judge, by the way, was the King; and as he wore his crown over the wig, (look at the frontispiece if you want to see how he did it,) he did not look at all comfortable, and it was certainly not becoming.

СAnd thatТs the jurybox,Т thought Alice, Сand those twelve creatures,Т (she was obliged to say Сcreatures,Т you see, because some of them were animals, and some were birds,) СI suppose they are the jurors.Т She said this last word two or three times over to herself, being rather proud of it: for she thought, and rightly too, that very few little girls of her age knew the meaning of it at all. However, СjurymenТ would have done just as well.

The twelve jurors were all writing very busily on slates. СWhat are they doing?Т Alice whispered to the Gryphon. СThey canТt have anything to put down yet, before the trialТs begun.Т

СTheyТre putting down their names,Т the Gryphon whispered in reply, Сfor fear they should forget them before the end of the trial.Т

СStupid things!Т Alice began in a loud, indignant voice, but she stopped hastily, for the White Rabbit cried out, СSilence in the court!Т and the King put on his spectacles and looked anxiously round, to make out who was talking.

Alice could see, as well as if she were looking over their shoulders, that all the jurors were writing down Сstupid things!Т on their slates, and she could even make out that one of them didnТt know how to spell Сstupid,Т and that he had to ask his neighbour to tell him. СA nice muddle their slatesТll be in before the trialТs over!Т thought Alice.

One of the jurors had a pencil that squeaked. This of course, Alice could not stand, and she went round the court and got behind him, and very soon found an opportunity of taking it away. She did it so quickly that the poor little juror (it was Bill, the Lizard) could not make out at all what had become of it; so, after hunting all about for it, he was obliged to write with one finger for the rest of the day; and this was of very little use, as it left no mark on the slate.

СHerald, read the accusation!Т said the King.

On this the White Rabbit blew three blasts on the trumpet, and then unrolled the parchment scroll, and read as follows: Ц

		СThe Queen of Hearts, she made some tarts,[45 - The Queen of hearts, she made some tartsЕ Ц Ёто не пароди€, а существующее детское стихотворение (автор неизвестен).]
		$$$$All on a summer day:
		The Knave of Hearts, he stole those tarts,
		$$$$And took them quite away!Т

СConsider your verdict,Т the King said to the jury.

СNot yet, not yet!Т the Rabbit hastily interrupted. СThereТs a great deal to come before that!Т

СCall the first witness,Т said the King; and the White Rabbit blew three blasts on the trumpet, and called out, СFirst witness!Т

The first witness was the Hatter. He came in with a teacup in one hand and a piece of breadandbutter in the other. СI beg pardon, your Majesty,Т he began, Сfor bringing these in: but I hadnТt quite finished my tea when I was sent for.Т

СYou ought to have finished,Т said the King. СWhen did you begin?Т

The Hatter looked at the March Hare, who had followed him into the court, arminarm with the Dormouse. СFourteenth of March, I think it was,Т he said.

СFifteenth,Т said the March Hare.

СSixteenth,Т added the Dormouse.

СWrite that down,Т the King said to the jury, and the jury eagerly wrote down all three dates on their slates, and then added them up, and reduced the answer to shillings and pence.

СTake off your hat,Т the King said to the Hatter.

СI keep them to sell,Т the Hatter added as an explanation; СIТve none of my own. IТm a hatter.Т

Here the Queen put on her spectacles, and began staring at the Hatter, who turned pale and fidgeted.

СGive your evidence,Т said the King; Сand donТt be nervous, or IТll have you executed on the spot.Т

This did not seem to encourage the witness at all: he kept shifting from one foot to the other, looking uneasily at the Queen, and in his confusion he bit a large piece out of his teacup instead of the breadandbutter.

Just at this moment Alice felt a very curious sensation, which puzzled her a good deal until she made out what it was: she was beginning to grow larger again, and she thought at first she would get up and leave the court; but on second thoughts she decided to remain where she was as long as there was room for her.

СI wish you wouldnТt squeeze so.Т said the Dormouse, who was sitting next to her. СI can hardly breathe.Т

СI canТt help it,Т said Alice very meekly: СIТm growing.Т

СYouТve no right to grow here,Т said the Dormouse.

СDonТt talk nonsense,Т said Alice more boldly: Сyou know youТre growing too.Т

СYes, but I grow at a reasonable pace,Т said the Dormouse: Сnot in that ridiculous fashion.Т And he got up very sulkily and crossed over to the other side of the court.

All this time the Queen had never left off staring at the Hatter, and, just as the Dormouse crossed the court, she said to one of the officers of the court, СBring me the list of the singers in the last concert!Т on which the wretched Hatter trembled so, that he shook both his shoes off.

СGive your evidence,Т the King repeated angrily, Сor IТll have you executed, whether youТre nervous or not.Т

СIТm a poor man, your Majesty,Т the Hatter began, in a trembling voice, СЦand I hadnТt begun my teaЦnot above a week or so Ц and what with the breadandbutter getting so thin Ц and the twinkling of the teaЦТ

СThe twinkling of the what?Т said the King.

СIt began with the tea,Т the Hatter replied.

СOf course twinkling begins with a T!Т said the King sharply. СDo you take me for a dunce? Go on!Т

СIТm a poor man,Т the Hatter went on, Сand most things twinkled after that Ц only the March Hare saidЦТ

СI didnТt!Т the March Hare interrupted in a great hurry.

СYou did!Т said the Hatter.

СI deny it!Т said the March Hare.

СHe denies it,Т said the King: Сleave out that part.Т

СWell, at any rate, the Dormouse saidЦТ the Hatter went on, looking anxiously round to see if he would deny it too: but the Dormouse denied nothing, being fast asleep.

СAfter that,Т continued the Hatter, СI cut some more breadandbutterЦТ

СBut what did the Dormouse say?Т one of the jury asked.

СThat I canТt remember,Т said the Hatter.

СYou MUST remember,Т remarked the King, Сor IТll have you executed.Т

The miserable Hatter dropped his teacup and breadandbutter, and went down on one knee. СIТm a poor man, your Majesty,Т he began.

СYouТre a very poor speaker,Т said the King.

Here one of the guineapigs cheered, and was immediately suppressed by the officers of the court. (As that is rather a hard word, I will just explain to you how it was done. They had a large canvas bag, which tied up at the mouth with strings: into this they slipped the guineapig, head first, and then sat upon it.)

СIТm glad IТve seen that done,Т thought Alice. СIТve so often read in the newspapers, at the end of trials, УThere was some attempts at applause, which was immediately suppressed by the officers of the court,Ф and I never understood what it meant till now.Т

СIf thatТs all you know about it, you may stand down,Т continued the King.

СI canТt go no lower,Т said the Hatter: СIТm on the floor, as it is.Т

СThen you may SIT down,Т the King replied.

Here the other guineapig cheered, and was suppressed.

СCome, that finished the guineapigs!Т thought Alice. СNow we shall get on better.Т

СIТd rather finish my tea,Т said the Hatter, with an anxious look at the Queen, who was reading the list of singers.

СYou may go,Т said the King, and the Hatter hurriedly left the court, without even waiting to put his shoes on.

СЦand just take his head off outside,Т the Queen added to one of the officers: but the Hatter was out of sight before the officer could get to the door.

СCall the next witness!Т said the King.

The next witness was the DuchessТs cook. She carried the pepperbox in her hand, and Alice guessed who it was, even before she got into the court, by the way the people near the door began sneezing all at once.

СGive your evidence,Т said the King.

СShanТt,Т said the cook.

The King looked anxiously at the White Rabbit, who said in a low voice, СYour Majesty must crossexamine THIS witness.Т

СWell, if I must, I must,Т the King said, with a melancholy air, and, after folding his arms and frowning at the cook till his eyes were nearly out of sight, he said in a deep voice, СWhat are tarts made of?Т

СPepper, mostly,Т said the cook.

СTreacle,Т said a sleepy voice behind her.

СCollar that Dormouse,Т the Queen shrieked out. СBehead that Dormouse! Turn that Dormouse out of court! Suppress him! Pinch him! Off with his whiskers!Т

For some minutes the whole court was in confusion, getting the Dormouse turned out, and, by the time they had settled down again, the cook had disappeared.

СNever mind!Т said the King, with an air of great relief. СCall the next witness.Т And he added in an undertone to the Queen, СReally, my dear, YOU must crossexamine the next witness. It quite makes my forehead ache!Т

Alice watched the White Rabbit as he fumbled over the list, feeling very curious to see what the next witness would be like, СЦfor they havenТt got much evidence YET,Т she said to herself. Imagine her surprise, when the White Rabbit read out, at the top of his shrill little voice, the name СAlice!Т




Chapter XII

AliceТs Evidence


СHere!Т cried Alice, quite forgetting in the flurry of the moment how large she had grown in the last few minutes, and she jumped up in such a hurry that she tipped over the jurybox with the edge of her skirt, upsetting all the jurymen on to the heads of the crowd below, and there they lay sprawling about, reminding her very much of a globe of goldfish she had accidentally upset the week before.

СOh, I BEG your pardon!Т she exclaimed in a tone of great dismay, and began picking them up again as quickly as she could, for the accident of the goldfish kept running in her head, and she had a vague sort of idea that they must be collected at once and put back into the jurybox, or they would die.

СThe trial cannot proceed,Т said the King in a very grave voice, Сuntil all the jurymen are back in their proper places Ц ALL,Т he repeated with great emphasis, looking hard at Alice as he said do.

Alice looked at the jurybox, and saw that, in her haste, she had put the Lizard in head downwards, and the poor little thing was waving its tail about in a melancholy way, being quite unable to move. She soon got it out again, and put it right; Сnot that it signifies much,Т she said to herself; СI should think it would be QUITE as much use in the trial one way up as the other.Т

As soon as the jury had a little recovered from the shock of being upset, and their slates and pencils had been found and handed back to them, they set to work very diligently to write out a history of the accident, all except the Lizard, who seemed too much overcome to do anything but sit with its mouth open, gazing up into the roof of the court.

СWhat do you know about this business?Т the King said to Alice.

СNothing,Т said Alice.

СNothing WHATEVER?Т persisted the King.

СNothing whatever,Т said Alice.

СThatТs very important,Т the King said, turning to the jury. They were just beginning to write this down on their slates, when the White Rabbit interrupted: СUNimportant, your Majesty means, of course,Т he said in a very respectful tone, but frowning and making faces at him as he spoke.

СUNimportant, of course, I meant,Т the King hastily said, and went on to himself in an undertone, Сimportant Ц unimportant Ц unimportant Ц importantЦТ as if he were trying which word sounded best.

Some of the jury wrote it down Сimportant,Т and some Сunimportant.Т Alice could see this, as she was near enough to look over their slates; Сbut it doesnТt matter a bit,Т she thought to herself.

At this moment the King, who had been for some time busily writing in his notebook, cackled out СSilence!Т and read out from his book, СRule Fortytwo. ALL PERSONS MORE THAN A MILE HIGH TO LEAVE THE COURT.Т

Everybody looked at Alice.

СIТM not a mile high,Т said Alice.

СYou are,Т said the King.

СNearly two miles high,Т added the Queen.

СWell, I shanТt go, at any rate,Т said Alice: Сbesides, thatТs not a regular rule: you invented it just now.Т

СItТs the oldest rule in the book,Т said the King.

СThen it ought to be Number One,Т said Alice.

The King turned pale, and shut his notebook hastily. СConsider your verdict,Т he said to the jury, in a low, trembling voice.

СThereТs more evidence to come yet, please your Majesty,Т said the White Rabbit, jumping up in a great hurry; Сthis paper has just been picked up.Т

СWhatТs in it?Т said the Queen.

СI havenТt opened it yet,Т said the White Rabbit, Сbut it seems to be a letter, written by the prisoner toЦto somebody.Т

СIt must have been that,Т said the King, Сunless it was written to nobody, which isnТt usual, you know.Т

СWho is it directed to?Т said one of the jurymen.

СIt isnТt directed at all,Т said the White Rabbit; Сin fact, thereТs nothing written on the OUTSIDE.Т He unfolded the paper as he spoke, and added СIt isnТt a letter, after all: itТs a set of verses.Т

СAre they in the prisonerТs handwriting?Т asked another of they jurymen.

СNo, theyТre not,Т said the White Rabbit, Сand thatТs the queerest thing about it.Т (The jury all looked puzzled.)

СHe must have imitated somebody elseТs hand,Т said the King. (The jury all brightened up again.)

СPlease your Majesty,Т said the Knave, СI didnТt write it, and they canТt prove I did: thereТs no name signed at the end.Т

СIf you didnТt sign it,Т said the King, Сthat only makes the matter worse. You MUST have meant some mischief, or else youТd have signed your name like an honest man.Т

There was a general clapping of hands at this: it was the first really clever thing the King had said that day.

СThat PROVES his guilt,Т said the Queen.

СIt proves nothing of the sort!Т said Alice. СWhy, you donТt even know what theyТre about!Т

СRead them,Т said the King.

The White Rabbit put on his spectacles. СWhere shall I begin, please your Majesty?Т he asked.

СBegin at the beginning,Т the King said gravely, Сand go on till you come to the end: then stop.Т

These were the verses the White Rabbit read: Ц

		SheТs all my fancy painted him[46 - SheТs all my fancy painted him Ц пароди€ наве€на стихотворением ”иль€ма ћи ЂЁлис √рейї.]
		(I make no idle boast);
		If he or you had lost a limb,
		Which would have suffered most?

		They told me you had been to her,
		And mentioned me to him:
		She gave me a good character,
		But said I could not swim.

		For this must ever be
		A secret, kept from all the rest,
		Between yourself and me.Т

СThatТs the most important piece of evidence weТve heard yet,Т said the King, rubbing his hands; Сso now let the juryЦТ

СIf any one of them can explain it,Т said Alice, (she had grown so large in the last few minutes that she wasnТt a bit afraid of interrupting him,) СIТll give him sixpence. I donТt believe thereТs an atom of meaning in it.Т

The jury all wrote down on their slates, СSHE doesnТt believe thereТs an atom of meaning in it,Т but none of them attempted to explain the paper.

СIf thereТs no meaning in it,Т said the King, Сthat saves a world of trouble, you know, as we neednТt try to find any. And yet I donТt know,Т he went on, spreading out the verses on his knee, and looking at them with one eye; СI seem to see some meaning in them, after all. УЦSAID I COULD NOT SWIMЦФ you canТt swim, can you?Т he added, turning to the Knave.

The Knave shook his head sadly. СDo I look like it?Т he said. (Which he certainly did NOT, being made entirely of cardboard.)

СAll right, so far,Т said the King, and he went on muttering over the verses to himself: СУWE KNOW IT TO BE TRUEЦФ thatТs the jury, of course Ц УI GAVE HER ONE, THEY GAVE HIM TWOЦФ why, that must be what he did with the tarts, you knowЦТ

СBut, it goes on УTHEY ALL RETURNED FROM HIM TO YOU,ФТ said Alice.

СWhy, there they are!Т said the King triumphantly, pointing to the tarts on the table. СNothing can be clearer than THAT. Then again Ц УBEFORE SHE HAD THIS FITЦФ you never had fits, my dear, I think?Т he said to the Queen.

СNever!Т said the Queen furiously, throwing an inkstand at the Lizard as she spoke. (The unfortunate little Bill had left off writing on his slate with one finger, as he found it made no mark; but he now hastily began again, using the ink, that was trickling down his face, as long as it lasted.)

СThen the words donТt FIT you,Т said the King, looking round the court with a smile. There was a dead silence.

СItТs a pun!Т the King added in an offended tone, and everybody laughed, СLet the jury consider their verdict,Т the King said, for about the twentieth time that day.

СNo, no!Т said the Queen. СSentence first Ц verdict afterwards.Т

СStuff and nonsense!Т said Alice loudly. СThe idea of having the sentence first!Т

СHold your tongue!Т said the Queen, turning purple.

СI wonТt!Т said Alice.

СOff with her head!Т the Queen shouted at the top of her voice. Nobody moved.

СWho cares for you?Т said Alice, (she had grown to her full size by this time.) СYouТre nothing but a pack of cards!Т

At this the whole pack rose up into the air, and came flying down upon her: she gave a little scream, half of fright and half of anger, and tried to beat them off, and found herself lying on the bank, with her head in the lap of her sister, who was gently brushing away some dead leaves that had fluttered down from the trees upon her face.

СWake up, Alice dear!Т said her sister; СWhy, what a long sleep youТve had!Т

СOh, IТve had such a curious dream!Т said Alice, and she told her sister, as well as she could remember them, all these strange Adventures of hers that you have just been reading about; and when she had finished, her sister kissed her, and said, СIt WAS a curious dream, dear, certainly: but now run in to your tea; itТs getting late.Т So Alice got up and ran off, thinking while she ran, as well she might, what a wonderful dream it had been.

But her sister sat still just as she left her, leaning her head on her hand, watching the setting sun, and thinking of little Alice and all her wonderful Adventures, till she too began dreaming after a fashion, and this was her dream: Ц

First, she dreamed of little Alice herself, and once again the tiny hands were clasped upon her knee, and the bright eager eyes were looking up into hersЦshe could hear the very tones of her voice, and see that queer little toss of her head to keep back the wandering hair that WOULD always get into her eyes Ц and still as she listened, or seemed to listen, the whole place around her became alive the strange creatures of her little sisterТs dream.

The long grass rustled at her feet as the White Rabbit hurried by Ц the frightened Mouse splashed his way through the neighbouring pool Ц she could hear the rattle of the teacups as the March Hare and his friends shared their neverending meal, and the shrill voice of the Queen ordering off her unfortunate guests to execution Ц once more the pigbaby was sneezing on the DuchessТs knee, while plates and dishes crashed around it Ц once more the shriek of the Gryphon, the squeaking of the LizardТs slatepencil, and the choking of the suppressed guineapigs, filled the air, mixed up with the distant sobs of the miserable Mock Turtle.

So she sat on, with closed eyes, and half believed herself in Wonderland, though she knew she had but to open them again, and all would change to dull reality Ц the grass would be only rustling in the wind, and the pool rippling to the waving of the reeds Ц the rattling teacups would change to tinkling sheepbells, and the QueenТs shrill cries to the voice of the shepherd boy Ц and the sneeze of the baby, the shriek of the Gryphon, and all thy other queer noises, would change (she knew) to the confused clamour of the busy farmyard Ц while the lowing of the cattle in the distance would take the place of the Mock TurtleТs heavy sobs.

Lastly, she pictured to herself how this same little sister of hers would, in the aftertime, be herself a grown woman; and how she would keep, through all her riper years, the simple and loving heart of her childhood: and how she would gather about her other little children, and make THEIR eyes bright and eager with many a strange tale, perhaps even with the dream of Wonderland of long ago: and how she would feel with all their simple sorrows, and find a pleasure in all their simple joys, remembering her own childlife, and the happy summer days.

Chapter I

Looking-Glass House


One thing was certain, that the white kitten had had nothing to do with it Ц it was the black kittenТs fault entirely. For the white kitten had been having its face washed by the old cat for the last quarter of an hour (and bearing it pretty well, considering): so you see that it couldnТt have had any hand in the mischief.

The way Dinah washed her childrenТs faces was this: first she held the poor thing down by its ear with one paw, and then with the other paw she rubbed its face all over, the wrong way, beginning at the nose: and just now, as I said, she was hard at work on the white kitten, which was lying quite still and trying to purr Ц no doubt feeling that it was all meant for its good.

But the black kitten had been finished with earlier in the afternoon, and so, while Alice was sitting curled up in a corner of the great armchair, half talking to herself and half asleep, the kitten had been having a grand game of romps with the ball of worsted Alice had been trying to wind up, and had been rolling it up and down till it had all come undone again; and there it was, spread over the hearthrug, all knots and tangles, with the kitten running after its own tail in the middle.

СOh, you wicked little thing!Т cried Alice, catching up the kitten, and giving it a little kiss to make it understand that it was in disgrace. СReally, Dinah ought to have taught you better manners! You ought, Dinah, you know you ought!Т she added, looking reproachfully at the old cat, and speaking in as cross a voice as she could manage Ц and then she scrambled back into the armchair, taking the kitten and the worsted with her, and began winding up the ball again. But she didnТt get on very fast, as she was talking all the time, sometimes to the kitten, and sometimes to herself. Kitty sat very demurely on her knee, pretending to watch the progress of the winding, and now and then putting out one paw and gently touching the ball, as if it would be glad to help, if it might.

СDo you know what tomorrow is, Kitty?Т[47 - —ледующий день Ц это 5 но€бр€, то есть день √а€ ‘окса. √ай ‘окс был участником порохового заговора Ц неудавшейс€ попытки взорвать здание ѕарламента вместе с королем. 5 но€бр€ 1605 г. заговор был раскрыт.  аждый год этот день отмечают кострами и фейерверками.] Alice began. СYouТd have guessed if youТd been up in the window with me Ц only Dinah was making you tidy, so you couldnТt. I was watching the boys getting in sticks for the bonfire Ц and it wants plenty of sticks, Kitty! Only it got so cold, and it snowed so, they had to leave off. Never mind, Kitty, weТll go and see the bonfire tomorrow.Т Here Alice wound two or three turns of the worsted round the kittenТs neck, just to see how it would look: this led to a scramble, in which the ball rolled down upon the floor, and yards and yards of it got unwound again.

СDo you know, I was so angry, Kitty,Т Alice went on, as soon as they were comfortably settled again, Сwhen I saw all the mischief you had been doing, I was very nearly opening the window, and putting you out into the snow! And youТd have deserved it, you little mischievous darling! What have you got to say for yourself? Now donТt interrupt me!Т she went on, holding up one finger. СIТm going to tell you all your faults. Number one: you squeaked twice while Dinah was washing your face this morning. Now you canТt deny it, Kitty: I heard you! What that you say?Т (pretending that the kitten was speaking.) СHer paw went into your eye? Well, thatТs your fault, for keeping your eyes open Ц if youТd shut them tight up, it wouldnТt have happened. Now donТt make any more excuses, but listen! Number two: you pulled Snowdrop away by the tail just as I had put down the saucer of milk before her! What, you were thirsty, were you? How do you know she wasnТt thirsty too? Now for number three: you unwound every bit of the worsted while I wasnТt looking!

СThatТs three faults, Kitty, and youТve not been punished for any of them yet. You know IТm saving up all your punishments for Wednesday week[48 - For Wednesday week Ц букв. до среды через среду; зд.: после дождичка в четверг (в день, который никогда не наступит).] Ц Suppose they had saved up all my punishments?Т she went on, talking more to herself than the kitten. СWhat would they do at the end of a year? I should be sent to prison, I suppose, when the day came. Or Ц let me see Ц suppose each punishment was to be going without a dinner: then, when the miserable day came, I should have to go without fifty dinners at once! Well, I shouldnТt mind that much! IТd far rather go without them than eat them!

СDo you hear the snow against the windowpanes, Kitty? How nice and soft it sounds! Just as if some one was kissing the window all over outside. I wonder if the snow loves the trees and fields, that it kisses them so gently? And then it covers them up snug, you know, with a white quilt; and perhaps it says, УGo to sleep, darlings, till the summer comes again.Ф And when they wake up in the summer, Kitty, they dress themselves all in green, and dance about Ц whenever the wind blows Ц oh, thatТs very pretty!Т cried Alice, dropping the ball of worsted to clap her hands. СAnd I do so wish it was true! IТm sure the woods look sleepy in the autumn, when the leaves are getting brown.

СKitty, can you play chess? Now, donТt smile, my dear, IТm asking it seriously. Because, when we were playing just now, you watched just as if you understood it: and when I said УCheck!Ф you purred! Well, it was a nice check, Kitty, and really I might have won, if it hadnТt been for that nasty Knight, that came wiggling down among my pieces. Kitty, dear, letТs pretend Ц СAnd here I wish I could tell you half the things Alice used to say, beginning with her favourite phrase СLetТs pretend.Т She had had quite a long argument with her sister only the day before Ц all because Alice had begun with СLetТs pretend weТre kings and queens;Т and her sister, who liked being very exact, had argued that they couldnТt, because there were only two of them, and Alice had been reduced at last to say, СWell, you can be one of them then, and IТll be all the rest.Т And once she had really frightened her old nurse by shouting suddenly in her ear, СNurse! Do letТs pretend that IТm a hungry hy&#230;na, and youТre a bone.Т

But this is taking us away from AliceТs speech to the kitten. СLetТs pretend that youТre the Red Queen, Kitty! Do you know, I think if you sat up and folded your arms, youТd look exactly like her. Now do try, thereТs a dear!Т And Alice got the Red Queen off the table, and set it up before the kitten as a model for it to imitate: however, the thing didnТt succeed, principally, Alice said, because the kitten wouldnТt fold its arms properly. So, to punish it, she held it up to the Lookingglass, that it might see how sulky it was СЦand if youТre not good directly,Т she added, СIТll put you through into Lookingglass House. How would you like that?

СNow, if youТll only attend, Kitty, and not talk so much, IТll tell you all my ideas about Lookingglass House. First, thereТs the room you can see through the glass Ц thatТs just the same as our drawingroom, only the things go the other way. I can see all of it when I get upon a chair Ц all but the bit behind the fireplace. Oh! I do so wish I could see that bit! I want so much to know whether theyТve a fire in the winter: you never can tell, you know, unless our fire smokes, and then smoke comes up in that room too Ц but that may be only pretence, just to make it look as if they had a fire. Well then, the books are something like our books, only the words go the wrong way: I know that, because IТve held up one of our books to the glass, and then they hold up one in the other room.

СHow would you like to live in Lookingglass House, Kitty? I wonder if theyТd give you milk in there? Perhaps Lookingglass milk isnТt good to drink Ц but oh, Kitty! now we come to the passage. You can just see a little peep of the passage in Lookingglass House, if you leave the door of our drawingroom wide open: and itТs very like our passage as far as you can see, only you know it may be quite different on beyond. Oh, Kitty, how nice it would be if we could only get through into Lookingglass House! IТm sure itТs got, oh! such beautiful things in it! LetТs pretend thereТs a way of getting through into it, somehow, Kitty. LetТs pretend the glass has got all soft like gauze, so that we can get through. Why, itТs turning into a sort of mist now, I declare! ItТll be easy enough to get throughЦТ She was up on the chimneypiece while she said this, though she hardly knew how she had got there. And certainly the glass was beginning to melt away, just like a bright silvery mist.

In another moment Alice was through the glass, and had jumped lightly down into the Lookingglass room. The very first thing she did was to look whether there was a fire in the fireplace, and she was quite pleased to find that there was a real one, blazing away as brightly as the one she had left behind. СSo I shall be as warm here as I was in the old room,Т thought Alice: Сwarmer, in fact, because thereТll be no one here to scold me away from the fire.[49 - ¬ викторианской Ѕритании огонь в спальн€х разводили только когда было действительно холодно. ¬ прохладную погоду самым теплым местом в доме была гостина€, в которой горел камин. ќднако считалось, что дет€м там делать нечего: им следовало проводить врем€ в детской, классной комнате и в саду.] Oh, what fun itТll be, when they see me through the glass in here, and canТt get at me!Т

Then she began looking about, and noticed that what could be seen from the old room was quite common and uninteresting, but that all the rest was as different as possible. For instance, the pictures on the wall next the fire seemed to be all alive, and the very clock on the chimneypiece (you know you can only see the back of it in the Lookingglass) had got the face of a little old man, and grinned at her.

СThey donТt keep this room so tidy as the other,Т Alice thought to herself, as she noticed several of the chessmen down in the hearth among the cinders; but in another moment, with a little СOh!Т of surprise, she was down on her hands and knees watching them. The chessmen were walking about, two and two!

СHere are the Red King and the Red Queen,Т Alice said (in a whisper, for fear of frightening them), Сand there are the White King and the White Queen sitting on the edge of the shovel Ц and here are two Castles walking arm in arm Ц I donТt think they can hear me,Т she went on, as she put her head closer down, Сand IТm nearly sure they canТt see me. I feel somehow as if I were invisibleЦТ

Here something began squeaking on the table behind Alice, and made her turn her head just in time to see one of the White Pawns roll over and begin kicking: she watched it with great curiosity to see what would happen next.

СIt is the voice of my child!Т the White Queen cried out, as she rushed past the King, so violently that she knocked him over among the cinders. СMy precious Lily! My imperial kitten!Т and she began scrambling wildly up the side of the fender.

СImperial fiddlestick!Т said the King, rubbing his nose, which had been hurt by the fall. He had a right to be a little annoyed with the Queen, for he was covered with ashes from head to foot.

Alice was very anxious to be of use, and, as the poor little Lily was nearly screaming herself into a fit, she hastily picked up the Queen and set her on the table by the side of her noisy little daughter.

The Queen gasped, and sat down: the rapid journey through the air had quite taken away her breath and for a minute or two she could do nothing but hug the little Lily in silence. As soon as she had recovered her breath a little, she called out to the White King, who was sitting sulkily among the ashes, СMind the volcano!Т

СWhat volcano?Т said the King, looking up anxiously into the fire, as if he thought that was the most likely place to find one.

СBlew Ц me Ц up,Т panted the Queen, who was still a little out of breath. СMind you come up Ц the regular way Ц donТt get blown up!Т

Alice watched the White King as he slowly struggled up from bar to bar, till at last she said, СWhy, youТll be hours and hours getting to the table, at that rate. IТd far better help you, hadnТt I?Т But the King took no notice of the question: it was quite clear that he could neither hear her nor see her.

So Alice picked him up very gently, and lifted him across more slowly than she had lifted the Queen, that she mightnТt take his breath away; but, before she put him on the table, she thought she might as well dust him a little, he was so covered with ashes.

She said afterwards that she had never seen in all her life such a face as the King made, when he found himself held in the air by an invisible hand, and being dusted: he was far too much astonished to cry out, but his eyes and his mouth went on getting larger and larger, and rounder and rounder, till her hand shook so with laughing that she nearly let him drop upon the floor.

СOh! please donТt make such faces, my dear!Т she cried out, quite forgetting that the King couldnТt hear her. СYou make me laugh so that I can hardly hold you! And donТt keep your mouth so wide open! All the ashes will get into it Ц there, now I think youТre tidy enough!Т she added, as she smoothed his hair, and set him upon the table near the Queen.

The King immediately fell flat on his back, and lay perfectly still; and Alice was a little alarmed at what she had done, and went round the room to see if she could find any water to throw over him. However, she could find nothing but a bottle of ink, and when she got back with it she found he had recovered, and he and the Queen were talking together in a frightened whisper Ц so low, that Alice could hardly hear what they said.

The King was saying СI assure, you my dear, I turned cold to the very ends of my whiskers!Т

To which the Queen replied СYou havenТt got any whiskers.Т

СThe horror of that moment,Т the King went on, СI shall never, never forget!Т

СYou will, though,Т the Queen said, Сif you donТt make a memorandum of it.Т

Alice looked on with great interest as the King took an enormous memorandumbook out of his pocket, and began writing. A sudden thought struck her, and she took hold of the end of the pencil, which came some way over his shoulder, and began writing for him.

The poor King look puzzled and unhappy, and struggled with the pencil for some time without saying anything; but Alice was too strong for him, and at last he panted out, СMy dear! I really must get a thinner pencil. I canТt manage this one a bit: it writes all manner of things that I donТt intendЦТ

СWhat manner of things?Т said the Queen, looking over the book (in which Alice had put СThe White Knight is sliding down the poker. He balances very badlyТ[50 - Ѕелый рыцарь снова по€витс€ в √лаве 8. “ам читатель получит объ€снение его плохому равновесию.]) СThatТs not a memorandum of your feelings!Т

There was a book lying near Alice on the table, and while she sat watching the White King (for she was still a little anxious about him, and had the ink all ready to throw over him, in case he fainted again), she turned over the leaves, to find some part that she could read, С Ц for itТs all in some language I donТt know,Т she said to herself.

It was like this.

СIt seems very pretty,Т she said when she had finished it, Сbut itТs rather hard to understand!Т (You see she didnТt like to confess, even to herself, that she couldnТt make it out at all.) СSomehow it seems to fill my head with ideas Ц only I donТt exactly know what they are! However, somebody killed something: thatТs clear, at any rate ЦТ

СBut oh!Т thought Alice, suddenly jumping up, Сif I donТt make haste I shall have to go back through the Lookingglass, before IТve seen what the rest of the house is like! LetТs have a look at the garden first!Т She was out of the room in a moment, and ran down stairs Ц or, at least, it wasnТt exactly running, but a new invention of hers for getting down stairs quickly and easily, as Alice said to herself. She just kept the tips of her fingers on the handrail, and floated gently down without even touching the stairs with her feet: then she floated on through the hall, and would have gone straight out at the door in the same way, if she hadnТt caught hold of the doorpost. She was getting a little giddy with so much floating in the air, and was rather glad to find herself walking again in the natural way.




Chapter II

The Garden of Live Flowers


СI should see the garden far better,Т said Alice to herself, Сif I could get to the top of that hill: and hereТs a path that leads straight to it Ц at least, no, it doesnТt do that ЦТ (after going a few yards along the path, and turning several sharp corners), Сbut I suppose it will at last. But how curiously it twists! ItТs more like a corkscrew than a path! Well, this turn goes to the hill, I suppose Ц no, it doesnТt! This goes straight back to the house! Well then, IТll try it the other way.Т

And so she did: wandering up and down, and trying turn after turn, but always coming back to the house, do what she would. Indeed, once, when she turned a corner rather more quickly than usual, she ran against it before she could stop herself.

СItТs no use talking about it,Т Alice said, looking up at the house and pretending it was arguing with her. СIТm not going in again yet. I know I should have to get through the Lookingglass again Ц back into the old room Ц and thereТd be an end of all my adventures!Т

So, resolutely turning back upon the house, she set out once more down the path, determined to keep straight on till she got to the hill. For a few minutes all went on well, and she was just saying, СI really shall do it this timeЦТ when the path gave a sudden twist and shook itself (as she described it afterwards), and the next moment she found herself actually walking in at the door.

СOh, itТs too bad!Т she cried. СI never saw such a house for getting in the way! Never!Т

However, there was the hill full in sight, so there was nothing to be done but start again. This time she came upon a large flowerbed, with a border of daisies, and a willowtree growing in the middle.

СO Tigerlily,Т said Alice, addressing herself to one that was waving gracefully about in the wind, СI wish you could talk!Т

СWe can talk,Т said the Tigerlily, Сwhen thereТs anybody worth talking to.Т

Alice was so astonished that she could not speak for a minute: it quite seemed to take her breath away. At length, as the Tigerlily only went on waving about, she spoke again, in a timid voice Ц almost in a whisper. СAnd can all the flowers talk?Т

СAs well as you can,Т said the Tigerlily. СAnd a great deal louder.Т

СIt isnТt manners for us to begin, you know,Т said the Rose[53 - „асто утверждают, что под розой подразумеваетс€ младша€ сестра јлисы –ода.], Сand I really was wondering when youТd speak! Said I to myself, УHer face has got some sense in it, thought itТs not a clever one!Ф Still, youТre the right colour, and that goes a long way.Т

СI donТt care about the colour,Т the Tigerlily remarked. СIf only her petals curled up a little more, sheТd be all right.Т

Alice didnТt like being criticized, so she began asking questions. СArenТt you sometimes frightened at being planted out here, with nobody to take care of you?Т

СThereТs the tree in the middle,Т said the Rose. СWhat else is it good for?Т

СBut what could it do, if any danger came?Т Alice asked.

СIt says УBoughwough!Ф cried a Daisy. СThatТs why its branches are called boughs!Т

СDidnТt you know that?Т cried another Daisy, and here they all began shouting together, till the air seemed quite full of little shrill voices. СSilence, every one of you!Т cried the Tigerlily, waving itself passionately from side to side, and trembling with excitement. СThey know I canТt get at them!Т it panted, bending its quivering head towards Alice, Сor they wouldnТt dare to do it!Т

СNever mind!Т Alice said in a soothing tone, and, stooping down to the daisies, who were just beginning again, she whispered СIf you donТt hold your tongues, IТll pick you!Т

There was silence in a moment, and several of the pink daisies turned white.

СThatТs right!Т said the Tigerlily. СThe daisies are worst of all. When one speaks, they all begin together, and itТs enough to make one wither to hear the way they go on!Т

СHow is it you can all talk so nicely?Т Alice said, hoping to get it into a better temper by a compliment. СIТve been in many gardens before, but none of the flowers could talk.Т

СPut your hand down, and feel the ground,Т said the Tigerlily. СThen youТll know why.Т

Alice did so. СItТs very hard,Т she said; Сbut I donТt see what that has to do with it.Т

СIn most gardens,Т the Tigerlily said, Сthey make the beds too soft Ц so that the flowers are always asleep.Т

This sounded a very good reason, and Alice was quite pleased to know it. СI never thought of that before!Т she said.

СItТs my opinion that you never think at all,Т the Rose said, in a rather severe tone.

СI never saw anybody that looked stupider,Т a Violet[54 - »м€ ¬айолет носила сама€ младша€ сестра јлисы.] said, so suddenly, that Alice quite jumped; for it hadnТt spoken before.

СHold your tongue!Т cried the Tigerlily. СAs if you ever saw anybody! You keep your head under the leaves, and snore away there, till you know no more whatТs going on in the world, than if you were a bud!Т

СAre there any more people in the garden besides me?Т Alice said, not choosing to notice the RoseТs last remark.

СThereТs one other flower in the garden that can move about like you,Т said the Rose. СI wonder how you do itЦТ (СYouТre always wondering,Т said the Tigerlily), Сbut sheТs more bushy than you are.Т

СIs she like me?Т Alice asked eagerly, for the thought crossed her mind, СThereТs another little girl in the garden, somewhere!Т

СWell, she has the same awkward shape as you,Т the Rose said: Сbut sheТs redder Ц and her petals are shorter, I think.Т

СHer petals are done up close, almost like a dahlia,Т the Tigerlily interrupted: Сnot tumbled about anyhow, like yours.Т

СBut thatТs not your fault,Т the Rose added kindly. СYouТre beginning to fade, you know Ц and then one canТt help oneТs petals getting a little untidy.Т

Alice didnТt like this idea at all: so, to change the subject, she asked СDoes she ever come out here?Т

СI daresay youТll see her soon,Т said the Rose. СSheТs one of the kind that has nine spikes, you know.Т

СWhere does she wear them?Т Alice asked with some curiosity.

СWhy, all round her head, of course,Т the Rose replied. СI was wondering you hadnТt got some too. I thought it was the regular rule.Т

СSheТs coming!Т cried the Larkspur. СI hear her footstep, thump, thump, along the gravelwalk!Т

Alice looked round eagerly and found that it was the Red Queen. СSheТs grown a good deal!Т was her first remark. She had indeed: when Alice first found her in the ashes, she had been only three inches high Ц and here she was, half a head taller than Alice herself!

СItТs the fresh air that does it,Т said the Rose: Сwonderfully fine air it is, out here.Т

СI think IТll go and meet her,Т said Alice, for, though the flowers were interesting enough, she felt that it would be far grander to have a talk with a real Queen.

СYou canТt possibly do that,Т said the Rose: СI should advise you to walk the other way.Т

This sounded nonsense to Alice, so she said nothing, but set off at once towards the Red Queen. To her surprise, she lost sight of her in a moment, and found herself walking in at the frontdoor again.

A little provoked, she drew back, and, after looking everywhere for the Queen (whom she spied out at last, a long way off), she thought she would try the plan, this time, of walking in the opposite direction.

It succeeded beautifully. She had not been walking a minute before she found herself face to face with the Red Queen, and full in sight of the hill she had been so long aiming at.

СWhere do you come from?Т said the Red Queen. СAnd where are you going? Look up, speak nicely, and donТt twiddle your fingers all the time.Т

Alice attended to all these directions, and explained, as well as she could, that she had lost her way.

СI donТt know what you mean by your way,Т said the Queen: Сall the ways about here belong to me Ц but why did you come out here at all?Т she added in a kinder tone. СCurtsey while youТre thinking what to say. It saves time.Т[55 - ¬ ¬икторианскую эпоху даже дет€м полагалось говорить вежливо и разумно. ƒела€ книксен, можно было быстро обдумать ответ и подобрать нужные слова.]

Alice wondered a little at this, but she was too much in awe of the Queen to disbelieve it. СIТll try it when I go home,Т she thought to herself, Сthe next time IТm a little late for dinner.Т

СItТs time for you to answer now,Т the Queen said, looking at her watch: Сopen your mouth a little wider when you speak, and always say Уyour Majesty.ФТ

СI only wanted to see what the garden was like, your Majesty ЦТ

СThatТs right,Т said the Queen, patting her on the head, which Alice didnТt like at all: Сthough, when you say УgardenФ Ц IТve seen gardens, compare with which this would be a wilderness.Т

Alice didnТt dare to argue the point, but went on: С Ц and I thought IТd try and find my way to the top of that hill ЦТ

СWhen you say Уhill,ФТ the Queen interrupted, СI could show you hills, in comparison with which youТd call that a valley.Т

СNo, I shouldnТt,Т said Alice, surprised into contradicting her at last: Сa hill canТt be a valley, you know. That would be nonsense ЦТ

The Red Queen shook her head, СYou may call it УnonsenseФ if you like,Т she said, С but IТve heard nonsense, compared with which that would be as sensible as a dictionary!Т

Alice curtseyed again, as she was afraid from the QueenТs tone that she was a little offended: and they walked on in silence till they got to the top of the little hill.

For some minutes Alice stood without speaking, looking out in all directions over the country Ц and a most curious country it was. There were a number of tiny little brooks running straight across it from side to side, and the ground between was divided up into squares by a number of little green hedges, that reached from brook to brook.

СI declare itТs marked out just like a large chessboard!Т Alice said at last. СThere ought to be some men moving about somewhere Ц and so there are!Т She added in a tone of delight, and her heart began to beat quick with excitement as she went on. СItТs a great huge game of chess thatТs being played Ц all over the world Ц if this is the world at all, you know. Oh, what fun it is! How I wish I was one of them! I wouldnТt mind being a Pawn, if only I might join Ц though of course I should like to be a Queen, best.Т

She glanced rather shyly at the real Queen as she said this, but her companion only smiled pleasantly, and said, СThatТs easily managed. You can be the White QueenТs Pawn, if you like, as LilyТs too young to play; and youТre in the Second Square to began with: when you get to the Eighth Square youТll be a Queen ЦТ Just at this moment, somehow or other, they began to run.

Alice never could quite make out, in thinking it over afterwards, how it was that they began: all she remembers is, that they were running hand in hand, and the Queen went so fast that it was all she could do to keep up with her: and still the Queen kept crying СFaster! Faster!Т, but Alice felt she could not go faster, though she had not breath left to say so.

The most curious part of the thing was, that the trees and the other things round them never changed their places at all: however fast they went, they never seemed to pass anything. СI wonder if all the things move along with us?Т thought poor puzzled Alice. And the Queen seemed to guess her thoughts, for she cried, СFaster! DonТt try to talk!Т

Not that Alice had any idea of doing that. She felt as if she would never be able to talk again, she was getting so much out of breath: and still the Queen cried СFaster! Faster!Т, and dragged her along. СAre we nearly there?Т Alice managed to pant out at last.

СNearly there!Т the Queen repeated. СWhy, we passed it ten minutes ago! Faster!Т And they ran on for a time in silence, with the wind whistling in AliceТs ears, and almost blowing her hair off her head, she fancied.

СNow! Now!Т cried the Queen. СFaster! Faster!Т And they went so fast that at last they seemed to skim through the air, hardly touching the ground with their feet, till suddenly, just as Alice was getting quite exhausted, they stopped, and she found herself sitting on the ground, breathless and giddy.

The Queen propped her up against a tree, and said kindly, СYou may rest a little, now.Т

Alice looked round her in great surprise. СWhy, I do believe weТve been under this tree the whole time! EverythingТs just as it was!Т

СOf course it is,Т said the Queen. СWhat would you have it?Т

СWell, in our country,Т said Alice, still panting a little, СyouТd generally get to somewhere else Ц if you ran very fast for a long time, as weТve been doing.Т

СA slow sort of country!Т said the Queen. СNow, here, you see, it takes all the running you can do, to keep in the same place. If you want to get somewhere else, you must run at least twice as fast as that!Т

СIТd rather not try, please!Т said Alice. СIТm quite content to stay here Ц only I am so hot and thirsty!Т

СI know what youТd like!Т the Queen said goodnaturedly, taking a little box out of her pocket. СHave a biscuit?Т

Alice thought it would not be civil to say СNo,Т though it wasnТt at all what she wanted. So she took it, and ate it as well as she could: and it was very dry: and she thought she had never been so nearly choked in all her life.

СWhile youТre refreshing yourself,Т said the Queen, СIТll just take the measurements.Т And she took a ribbon out of her pocket, marked in inches, and began measuring the ground, and sticking little pegs in here and there.

СAt the end of two yards,Т she said, putting in a peg to mark the distance, СI shall give you your directions Ц have another biscuit?Т

СNo, thank you,Т said Alice: СoneТs quite enough!Т

СThirst quenched, I hope?Т said the Queen.

Alice did not know what to say to this, but luckily the Queen did not wait for an answer, but went on. СAt the end of three yards I shall repeat them Ц for fear of your forgetting them. At the end of four, I shall say goodbye. And at the end of five, I shall go!Т

She had got all the pegs put in by this time, and Alice looked on with great interest as she returned to the tree, and then began slowly walking down the row.

At the twoyard peg she faced round, and said СA pawn goes two squares in its first move, you know. So youТll go very quickly through the Third Square Ц by railway, I should think Ц and youТll find yourself in the Fourth Square in no time. Well, that square belongs to Tweedledum and Tweedledee Ц the Fifth is mostly water Ц the Sixth belongs to Humpty Dumpty Ц But you make no remark?Т

СI Ц I didnТt know I had to make one Ц just then,Т Alice faltered out.

СYou should have said,Т the Queen went on in a tone of great reproof, СУItТs extremely kind of you to tell me all thisФ Ц however, weТll suppose it said Ц the Seventh Square is all forest Ц however, one of the Knights will show you the way Ц and in the Eighth Square we shall be Queens together, and itТs all feasting and fun!Т Alice got up and curtseyed, and sat down again.

At the next peg the Queen turned again, and this time she said СSpeak in French when you canТt think of the English for a thing Ц turn out your toes as you walk Ц and remember who you are!Т She did not wait for Alice to curtsey, this time, but walked on quickly to the next peg, where she turned for a moment to say СGoodbye,Т and then hurried on to the last.

How it happened, Alice never knew, but exactly as she came to the last peg, she was gone. Whether she vanished into the air, or whether she ran quickly into the wood (Сand she can run very fast!Т thought Alice), there was no way of guessing, but she was gone, and Alice began to remember that she was a Pawn, and that it would soon be time for her to move.




Chapter III

Looking-Glass Insects


Of course the first thing to do was to make a grand survey of the country she was going to travel through. СItТs something very like learning geography,Т thought Alice, as she stood on tiptoe in hopes of being able to see a little further. СPrincipal rivers Ц there are none. Principal mountains Ц IТm on the only one, but I donТt think itТs got any name. Principal towns Ц why, what are those creatures, making honey down there? They canТt be bees Ц nobody ever saw bees a mile off, you know ЦТ and for some time she stood silent, watching one of them that was bustling about among the flowers, poking its proboscis[56 - Proboscis Ц хобот (у насекомых и также слоновий хобот).] into them, Сjust as if it was a regular bee,Т thought Alice.

However, this was anything but a regular bee: in fact, it was an elephant Ц as Alice soon found out, though the idea quite took her breath away at first. СAnd what enormous flowers they must be!Т was her next idea. СSomething like cottages with the roofs taken off, and stalks put to them Ц and what quantities of honey they must make! I think IТll go down and Ц no, I wonТt just yet,Т she went on, checking herself just as she was beginning to run down the hill, and trying to find some excuse for turning shy so suddenly. СItТll never do to go down among them without a good long branch to brush them away Ц and what fun itТll be when they ask me how I liked my walk. I shall say УOh, I like it well enough ЦФ (here came the favourite little toss of the head), СУonly it was so dusty and hot, and the elephants did tease so!Ф

СI think IТll go down the other way,Т she said after a pause; Сand perhaps I may visit the elephants later on. Besides, I do so want to get into the Third Square!Т

So, with this excuse, she ran down the hill and jumped over the first of the six little brooks.


* * *

СTickets, please!Т said the Guard, putting his head in at the window. In a moment everybody was holding out a ticket: they were about the same size as the people, and quite seemed to fill the carriage.

СNow then! Show your ticket, child!Т the Guard went on, looking angrily at Alice. And a great many voices all said together (Сlike the chorus of a song,Т thought Alice), СDonТt keep him waiting, child! Why, his time is worth a thousand pounds a minute!Т

СIТm afraid I havenТt got one,Т Alice said in a frightened tone: Сthere wasnТt a ticketoffice where I came from.Т And again the chorus of voices went on. СThere wasnТt room for one where she came from. The land there is worth a thousand pounds an inch!Т

СDonТt make excuses,Т said the Guard: Сyou should have bought one from the enginedriver.Т And once more the chorus of voices went on with СThe man that drives the engine. Why, the smoke alone is worth a thousand pounds a puff!Т

Alice thought to herself, СThen thereТs no use in speaking.Т The voices didnТt join in this time, as she hadnТt spoken, but, to her great surprise, they all thought in chorus (I hope you understand what thinking in chorus means Ц for I must confess that I donТt), СBetter say nothing at all. Language is worth a thousand pounds a word!Т

СI shall dream about a thousand pounds tonight, I know I shall!Т thought Alice.

All this time the Guard was looking at her, first through a telescope, then through a microscope, and then through an operaglass[57 - Operaglass(es) Ц театральный бинокль.]. At last he said, СYouТre travelling the wrong way,Т and shut up the window, and went away.

СSo young a child,Т said the gentleman sitting opposite to her, (he was dressed in white paper), Сought to know which way sheТs going, even if she doesnТt know her own name!Т

A Goat, that was sitting next to the gentleman in white, shut his eyes and said in a loud voice, СShe ought to know her way to the ticketoffice, even if she doesnТt know her alphabet!Т

There was a Beetle sitting next to the Goat (it was a very queer carriagefull of passengers altogether), and, as the rule seemed to be that they should all speak in turn, he went on with СSheТll have to go back from here as luggage!Т

Alice couldnТt see who was sitting beyond the Beetle, but a hoarse voice spoke next. СChange enginesТ Ц it said, and there it choked and was obliged to leave off.

СIt sounds like a horse,Т Alice thought to herself. And an extremely small voice, close to her ear, said СYou might make a joke on that Ц something about УhorseФ and Уhoarse,Ф you know.Т

Then a very gentle voice in the distance said, СShe must be labelled УLass, with care,Ф you knowЦТ

And after that other voices went on (СWhat a number of people there are in the carriage!Т thought Alice), saying СShe must go by post, as sheТs got a head on her ЦТ СShe must be sent as a message by the telegraph ЦТ СShe must draw the train herself the rest of the way Ц,Т and so on.

But the gentleman dressed in white paper leaned forwards and whispered in her ear, СNever mind what they all say, my dear, but take a returnticket every time the train stops.Т

СIndeed I shanТt!Т Alice said rather impatiently. СI donТt belong to this railway journey at all Ц I was in a wood just now Ц and I wish I could get back there.Т

СYou might make a joke on that,Т said the little voice close to her ear: Сsomething about Уyou would if you could,Ф you know.Т

СDonТt tease so,Т said Alice, looking about in vain to see where the voice came from. СIf youТre so anxious to have a joke made, why donТt you make one yourself?Т

The little voice sighed deeply. It was very unhappy, evidently, and Alice would have said something pitying to comfort it, Сif it would only sigh like other people!Т she thought. But this was such a wonderfully small sigh, that she wouldnТt have heard it at all, if it hadnТt come quite close to her ear. The consequence of this was that it tickled her ear very much, and quite took off her thoughts from the unhappiness of the poor little creature.

СI know you are a friend,Т the little voice went on: Сa dear friend, and an old friend. And you wonТt hurt me, though I am an insect.Т

СWhat kind of insect?Т Alice inquired, a little anxiously. What she really wanted to know was, whether it could sting or not, but she thought this wouldnТt be quite a civil question to ask.

СWhat, then you donТtЦТ the little voice began, when it was drowned by a shrill scream from the engine, and everybody jumped up in alarm, Alice among the rest.

The Horse, who had put his head out of the window, quietly drew it in and said СItТs only a brook we have to jump over.Т Everybody seemed satisfied with this, though Alice felt a little nervous at the idea of trains jumping at all. СHowever, itТll take us into the Fourth Square, thatТs some comfort!Т she said to herself. In another moment she felt the carriage rise straight up into the air, and in her fright she caught at the thing nearest to her hand, which happened to be the GoatТs beard.


* * *

But the beard seemed to melt away as she touched it, and she found herself sitting quietly under a tree Ц while the Gnat[58 - Gnat Ц разновидность комара.] (for that was the insect she had been talking to) was balancing itself on a twig just over her head, and fanning her with its wings.

It certainly was a very large Gnat: Сabout the size of a chicken,Т Alice thought. Still, she couldnТt feel nervous with it, after they had been talking together so long.

A new difficulty came into AliceТs head. СSupposing it couldnТt find any?Т she suggested.

After this, Alice was silent for a minute or two, pondering. The Gnat amused itself meanwhile by humming round and round her head: at last it settled again and remarked, СI suppose you donТt want to lose your name?Т

Then came another of those melancholy little sighs, and this time the poor Gnat really seemed to have sighed itself away, for, when Alice looked up, there was nothing whatever to be seen on the twig, and, as she was getting quite chilly with sitting still so long, she got up and walked on.

She very soon came to an open field, with a wood on the other side of it: it looked much darker than the last wood, and Alice felt a little timid about going into it. СThis must be the wood, she said thoughtfully to herself, Сwhere things have no names. I wonder whatТll become of my name when I go in? Ц јлиса права: ее фамили€ Ћидделл.]

Chapter IV

Tweedledum and Tweedledee

Alice laid her hand upon his arm, and said in a soothing tone, СYou neednТt be so angry about an old rattle.Т

СBut it isnТt old!Т Tweedledum cried, in a greater fury than ever. СItТs new, I tell you Ц I bought it yesterday Ц my nice NEW RATTLE!Т and his voice rose to a perfect scream.

All this time Tweedledee was trying his best to fold up the umbrella, with himself in it: which was such an extraordinary thing to do, that it quite took off AliceТs attention from the angry brother. But he couldnТt quite succeed, and it ended in his rolling over, bundled up in the umbrella, with only his head out: and there he lay, opening and shutting his mouth and his large eyes Ц Сlooking more like a fish than anything else,Т Alice thought.

СOf course you agree to have a battle?Т Tweedledum said in a calmer tone.

СI suppose so,Т the other sulkily replied, as he crawled out of the umbrella: Сonly she must help us to dress up, you know.Т

So the two brothers went off handinhand into the wood, and returned in a minute with their arms full of things Ц such as bolsters[67 - Bolster Ц длинна€ узка€ обычно кругла€ подушка.], blankets, hearthrugs, tablecloths, dishcovers and coalscuttles. СI hope youТre a good hand at pinning and tying strings?Т Tweedledum remarked. СEvery one of these things has got to go on, somehow or other.Т

Alice said afterwards she had never seen such a fuss made about anything in all her life Ц the way those two bustled about Ц and the quantity of things they put on Ц and the trouble they gave her in tying strings and fastening buttons Ц СReally theyТll be more like bundles of old clothes that anything else, by the time theyТre ready!Т she said to herself, as she arranged a bolster round the neck of Tweedledee, Сto keep his head from being cut off,Т as he said.

СYou know,Т he added very gravely, СitТs one of the most serious things that can possibly happen to one in a battle Ц to get oneТs head cut off.Т

Alice laughed loud: but she managed to turn it into a cough, for fear of hurting his feelings.

СDo I look very pale?Т said Tweedledum, coming up to have his helmet tied on. (He called it a helmet, though it certainly looked much more like a saucepan.)

СWell Ц yes Ц a little,Т Alice replied gently.

СIТm very brave generally,Т he went on in a low voice: Сonly today I happen to have a headache.Т

СAnd IТve got a toothache!Т said Tweedledee, who had overheard the remark. СIТm far worse off than you!Т

СThen youТd better not fight today,Т said Alice, thinking it a good opportunity to make peace.

СWe must have a bit of a fight, but I donТt care about going on long,Т said Tweedledum. СWhatТs the time now?Т

Tweedledee looked at his watch, and said СHalfpast four.Т

СLetТs fight till six, and then have dinner,Т said Tweedledum.

СVery well,Т the other said, rather sadly: Сand she can watch us Ц only youТd better not come very close,Т he added: СI generally hit everything I can see Ц when I get really excited.Т

СAnd I hit everything within reach,Т cried Tweedledum, Сwhether I can see it or not!Т

Alice laughed. СYou must hit the trees pretty often, I should think,Т she said.

Tweedledum looked round him with a satisfied smile. СI donТt suppose,Т he said, СthereТll be a tree left standing, for ever so far round, by the time weТve finished!Т

СAnd all about a rattle!Т said Alice, still hoping to make them a little ashamed of fighting for such a trifle.

СI shouldnТt have minded it so much,Т said Tweedledum, Сif it hadnТt been a new one.Т

СI wish the monstrous crow would come!Т though Alice.

СThereТs only one sword, you know,Т Tweedledum said to his brother: Сbut you can have the umbrella Ц itТs quite as sharp. Only we must begin quick. ItТs getting as dark as it can.Т

СAnd darker.Т said Tweedledee.

It was getting dark so suddenly that Alice thought there must be a thunderstorm coming on. СWhat a thick black cloud that is!Т she said. СAnd how fast it comes! Why, I do believe itТs got wings!Т

СItТs the crow!Т Tweedledum cried out in a shrill voice of alarm: and the two brothers took to their heels and were out of sight in a moment.

Alice ran a little way into the wood, and stopped under a large tree. СIt can never get at me here,Т she thought: СitТs far too large to squeeze itself in among the trees. But I wish it wouldnТt flap its wings so Ц it make quite a hurricane in the wood Ц hereТs somebodyТs shawl being blown away!Т




Chapter V

Wool and Water


She caught the shawl as she spoke, and looked about for the owner: in another moment the White Queen came running wildly through the wood, with both arms stretched out wide, as if she were flying, and Alice very civilly went to meet her with the shawl.

СIТm very glad I happened to be in the way,Т Alice said, as she helped her to put on her shawl again.

The While Queen only looked at her in a helpless frightened sort of way, and kept repeating something in a whisper to herself that sounded like Сbreadandbutter, breadandbutter,Т and Alice felt that if there was to be any conversation at all, she must manage it herself. So she began rather timidly: СAm I addressing the White Queen?Т

СWell, yes, if you call that adressing,Т The Queen said. СIt isnТt my notion of the thing, at all.Т

Alice thought it would never do to have an argument at the very beginning of their conversation, so she smiled and said, СIf your Majesty will only tell me the right way to begin, IТll do it as well as I can.Т

It would have been all the better, as it seemed to Alice, if she had got some one else to dress her, she was so dreadfully untidy. СEvery single thingТs crooked,Т Alice thought to herself, Сand sheТs all over pins! Ц May I put your shawl straight for you?Т she added aloud.

СIt canТt go straight, you know, if you pin it all on one side,Т Alice said, as she gently put it right for her; Сand, dear me, what a state your hair is in!Т

Alice carefully released the brush, and did her best to get the hair into order. СCome, you look rather better now!Т she said, after altering most of the pins. СBut really you should have a ladyТs maid[68 - LadyТs maid Ц горнична€ хоз€йки; занималась плать€ми и украшени€ми дамы, укладывала ей волосы, помогала одетьс€ и раздетьс€ (что в ¬икторианскую эпоху было не такто просто). ¬ иерархии слуг это была довольно высока€ должность.]!Т

СIТm sure IТll take you with pleasure!Т the Queen said. СTwopence a week, and jam every other day.Т

Alice couldnТt help laughing, as she said, СI donТt want you to hire me Ц and I donТt care for jam.Т

СItТs very good jam,Т said the Queen.

СWell, I donТt want any today, at any rate.Т

СIt must come sometimes to Уjam doday,Ф| Alice objected.

СNo, it canТt,Т said the Queen. СItТs jam every other day: today isnТt any other day, you know.Т

С Ц but thereТs one great advantage in it, that oneТs memory works both ways.Т

Alice felt there was no denying that. СOf course it would be all the better,Т she said: Сbut it wouldnТt be all the better his being punished.Т

Alice was just beginning to say СThereТs a mistake somewhere Ц,Т when the Queen began screaming so loud that she had to leave the sentence unfinished. СOh, oh, oh!Т shouted the Queen, shaking her hand about as if she wanted to shake it off. СMy fingerТs bleeding! Oh, oh, oh, oh!Т

Her screams were so exactly like the whistle of a steamengine, that Alice had to hold both her hands over her ears.

СIТm seven and a half exactly.Т

The brooch had come undone as she spoke, and a sudden gust of wind blew the QueenТs shawl across a little brook. * * *

СOh, much better!Т cried the Queen, her voice rising to a squeak as she went on. СMuch beetter! Beetter! Beeeetter! Beeehh!Т The last word ended in a long bleat, so like a sheep that Alice quite started.

She looked at the Queen, who seemed to have suddenly wrapped herself up in wool. Alice rubbed her eyes, and looked again. She couldnТt make out what had happened at all. Was she in a shop? And was that really Ц was it really a sheep that was sitting on the other side of the counter? Rub as she could, she could make nothing more of it: she was in a little dark shop, leaning with her elbows on the counter, and opposite to her was an old Sheep, sitting in an armchair knitting, and every now and then leaving off to look at her through a great pair of spectacles.

СWhat is it you want to buy?Т the Sheep said at last, looking up for a moment from her knitting.

СI donТt quite know yet,Т Alice said, very gently. СI should like to look all round me first, if I might.Т

СYou may look in front of you, and on both sides, if you like,Т said the Sheep; Сbut you canТt look all round you Ц unless youТve got eyes at the back of your head.Т

But these, as it happened, Alice had not got: so she contented herself with turning round, looking at the shelves as she came to them.

The shop seemed to be full of all manner of curious things Ц but the oddest part of it all was that, whenever she looked hard at any shelf, to make out exactly what it had on it, that particular shelf was always quite empty, though the others round it were crowded as full as they could hold.

СThings flow about so here!Т she said at last in a plaintive tone, after she had spent a minute or so in vainly pursuing a large bright thing, that looked sometimes like a doll and sometimes like a workbox, and was always in the shelf next above the one she was looking at. СAnd this one is the most provoking of all Ц but IТll tell you what ЦТ she added, as a sudden thought struck her. СIТll follow it up to the very top shelf of all. But even this plan failed: the СthingТ went through the ceiling as quietly as possible, as if it were quite used to it.

СAre you a child or a teetotum[69 - Teetotum Ц разновидность волчка: обычно квадратный, с подписанными на боках буквами и цифрами.]?Т the Sheep said, as she took up another pair of needles. СYouТll make me giddy soon, if you go on turning round like that.Т She was now working with fourteen pairs at once, and Alice couldnТt help looking at her in great astonishment.

СHow can she knit with so many?Т the puzzled child thought to herself. СShe gets more and more like a porcupine every minute!Т
