СYes, a little Ц but not on land Ц and not with needles Ц Т Alice was beginning to say, when suddenly the needles turned into oars in her hands, and she found they were in a little boat, gliding along between banks: so there was nothing for it but to do her best.

СFeather![70 - Feather! Ц «десь подразумеваетс€ не птичье перо, а приказ развернуть весло плашм€.]Т cried the Sheep, as she took up another pair of needles.

This didnТt sound like a remark that needed any answer: so Alice said nothing, but pulled away. There was something very queer about the water, she thought, as every now and then the oars got fast in it, and would hardly come out again.

СFeather! Feather!Т the Sheep cried again, taking more needles. СYouТll be catching a crab[71 - Catching a crab Ц to catch a crab означает Ђне мочь выдернуть неудачно опущенное весло из воды или грести по воздухуї.] directly.Т

СA dear little crab!Т thought Alice. СI should like that.Т

СIn the water, of course!Т said the Sheep, sticking some of the needles into her hair, as her hands were full. СFeather, I say!Т

СWhy do you say УFeatherФ so often?Т Alice asked at last, rather vexed. СIТm not a bird!Т

СYou are,Т said the Sheet: СyouТre a little goose[72 - Goose Ц это относительно м€гко сформулированное Ђдурачок/дурочкаї.].Т

This offended Alice a little, so there was no more conversation for a minute or two, while the boat glided gently on, sometimes among beds of weeds (which made the oars stick fast in the water, worse then ever), and sometimes under trees, but always with the same tall riverbanks frowning over their heads.

СOh, please! There are some scented rushes!Т Alice cried in a sudden transport of delight. СThere really are Ц and such beauties!Т

СYou neednТt say УpleaseФ to me about ТemТ the Sheep said, without looking up from her knitting: СI didnТt put Тem there, and IТm not going to take Тem away.Т

So the boat was left to drift down the stream as it would, till it glided gently in among the waving rushes. And then the little sleeves were carefully rolled up, and the little arms were plunged in elbowdeep to get the rushes a good long way down before breaking them off Ц and for a while Alice forgot all about the Sheep and the knitting, as she bent over the side of the boat, with just the ends of her tangled hair dipping into the water Ц while with bright eager eyes she caught at one bunch after another of the darling scented rushes.

СI only hope the boat wonТt tipple over!Т she said to herself. СOh, what a lovely one! СThe prettiest are always further!Т she said at last, with a sigh at the obstinacy of the rushes in growing so far off, as, with flushed cheeks and dripping hair and hands, she scrambled back into her place, and began to arrange her newfound treasures.

What mattered it to her just than that the rushes had begun to fade, and to lose all their scent and beauty, from the very moment that she picked them? Even real scented rushes, you know, last only a very little while Ц and these, being dreamrushes, melted away almost like snow, as they lay in heaps at her feet Ц but Alice hardly noticed this, there were so many other curious things to think about.

They hadnТt gone much farther before the blade of one of the oars got fast in the water and wouldnТt come out again (so Alice explained it afterwards), and the consequence was that the handle of it caught her under the chin, and, in spite of a series of little shrieks of СOh, oh, oh!Т from poor Alice, it swept her straight off the seat, and down among the heap of rushes.

However, she wasnТt a bit hurt, and was soon up again: the Sheep went on with her knitting all the while, just as if nothing had happened. СThat was a nice crab you caught!Т she remarked, as Alice got back into her place, very much relieved to find herself still in the boat.

СWas it? I didnТt see it,Т said Alice, peeping cautiously over the side of the boat into the dark water. СI wish it hadnТt let go Ц I should so like a little crab to take home with me!Т But the Sheep only laughed scornfully, and went on with her knitting.

СAre there many crabs here?Т said Alice.

СCrabs, and all sorts of things,Т said the Sheep: Сplenty of choice, only make up your mind. Now, what do you want to buy?Т

СTo buy!Т Alice echoed in a tone that was half astonished and half frightened Ц for the oars, and the boat, and the river, had vanished all in a moment, and she was back again in the little dark shop.

СI should like to buy an egg, please,Т she said timidly. СHow do you sell them?Т

СFivepence farthing for one Ц twopence for two,Т the Sheep replied.

СOnly you must eat them both, if you buy two,Т[73 - ѕо легенде, в некоторых заведени€х, если посетитель заказывал одно вареное €йцо, ему подавали сразу два, так как одно неизбежно оказывалось несвежим.] said the Sheep.

СThen IТll have one, please,Т said Alice, as she put the money down on the counter. For she thought to herself, СThey mightnТt be at all nice, you know.Т

The Sheep took the money, and put it away in a box: then she said СI never put things into peopleТs hands Ц that would never do Ц you must get it for yourself.Т And so saying, she went off to the other end of the shop, and set the egg upright on a shelf.

СI wonder why it wouldnТt do?Т thought Alice, as she groped her way among the tables and chairs, for the shop was very dark towards the end. СThe egg seems to get further away the more I walk towards it. Let me see, is this a chair? Why, itТs got branches, I declare! How very odd to find trees growing here! * * *

So she went on, wondering more and more at every step, as everything turned into a tree the moment she came up to it, and she quite expected the egg to do the same.




Chapter VI

Humpty Dumpty


However, the egg only got larger and larger, and more and more human: when she had come within a few yards of it, she saw that it had eyes and a nose and mouth; and when she had come close to it, she saw clearly that it was HUMPTY DUMPTY[74 - Humpty Dumpty Ц это персонаж старинного детского стихотворени€. ќбычно его описывают как человека€йцо. ќдни исследователи предполагают, что образ был наве€н исторической личностью, другие утверждают, что так была названа огромна€ пушка. ѕроисхождение персонажа точно не установлено.] himself. СIt canТt be anybody else!Т she said to herself. СIТm as certain of it, as if his name were written all over his face.Т

It might have been written a hundred times, easily, on that enormous face. Humpty Dumpty was sitting, with his legs crossed like a Turk, on the top of a high wall Ц such a narrow one that Alice quite wondered how he could keep his balance Ц and, as his eyes were steadily fixed in the opposite direction, and he didnТt take the least notice of her, she thought he must be a stuffed figure after all.

СAnd how exactly like an egg he is!Т she said aloud, standing with her hands ready to catch him, for she was every moment expecting him to fall.

СItТs very provoking,Т Humpty Dumpty said after a long silence, looking away from Alice as he spoke, Сto be called an egg Ц very!Т

СSome people,Т said Humpty Dumpty, looking away from her as usual, Сhave no more sense than a baby!Т

Alice didnТt know what to say to this: it wasnТt at all like conversation, she thought, as he never said anything to her; in fact, his last remark was evidently addressed to a tree Ц so she stood and softly repeated to herself: Ц

		СHumpty Dumpty sat on a wall:
		Humpty Dumpty had a great fall.
		All the KingТs horses and all the KingТs men
		CouldnТt put Humpty Dumpty in his place again.Т

СThat last line is much too long for the poetry,Т she added, almost out loud, forgetting that Humpty Dumpty would hear her.

СDonТt stand there chattering to yourself like that,Т Humpty Dumpty said, looking at her for the first time, Сbut tell me your name and your business.Т

СMy name is Alice, but ЦТ

СMust a name mean something?Т Alice asked doubtfully.

СOf course it must,Т Humpty Dumpty said with a sort laugh: Сmy name means the shape I am Ц and a good handsome shape it is, too. With a name like your, you might be any shape, almost.Т

СWhy do you sit out here all alone?Т said Alice, not wishing to begin an argument.

СWhat tremendously easy riddles you ask!Т Humpty Dumpty growled out. СOf course I donТt think so! Why, if ever I did fall off Ц which thereТs no chance of Ц but if I did ЦТ Here he pursed up his lips, and looked so solemn and grand that Alice could hardly help laughing. СIf I did fall,Т he went on, СThe King has promised me Ц ah, you may turn pale, if you like! You didnТt think I was going to say that, did you? The King has promised me Ц with his very own mouth Ц to Ц to ЦТ

СTo send all his horses and all his men,Т Alice interrupted, rather unwisely.

СNow I declare thatТs too bad!Т Humpty Dumpty cried, breaking into a sudden passion. СYouТve been listening at doors Ц and behind trees Ц and down chimneys Ц or you couldnТt have known it!Т

СI havenТt, indeed!Т Alice said very gently. СItТs in a book.Т

СAh, well! They may write such things in a book,Т Humpty Dumpty said in a calmer tone. СThatТs what you call a History of England, that is. Now, take a good look at me! IТm one that has spoken to a King, I am: mayhap youТll never see such another: and, to show you IТm not proud, you may shake hands with me!Т And he grinned almost from ear to ear, as he leant forwards (and as nearly as possible fell of the wall in doing so) and offered Alice his hand. She watched him a little anxiously as she took it. СIf he smiled much more, the ends of his mouth might meet behind,Т she thought: Сand then I donТt know what would happen to his head! IТm afraid it would come off!Т

СYes, all his horses and all his men,Т Humpty Dumpty went on. СTheyТd pick me up again in a minute, they would! However, this conversation is going on a little too fast: letТs go back to the last remark but one.Т

СIТm afraid I canТt quite remember it,Т Alice said very politely.

СIn that case we start afresh,Т said Humpty Dumpty, Сand itТs my turn to choose a subject ЦТ (СHe talks about it just as if it was a game!Т thought Alice.) СSo hereТs a question for you. How old did you say you were?Т

Alice made a short calculation, and said СSeven years and six months.Т

СWrong!Т Humpty Dumpty exclaimed triumphantly. СYou never said a word like it!Т

СI though you meant УHow old are you?ФТ Alice explained.

СIf IТd meant that, IТd have said it,Т said Humpty Dumpty.

Alice didnТt want to begin another argument, so she said nothing.

СSeven years and six months!Т Humpty Dumpty repeated thoughtfully. СAn uncomfortable sort of age. Now if youТd asked my advice, IТd have said УLeave off at sevenФ Ц but itТs too late now.Т

СI never ask advice about growing,Т Alice said indignantly.

СToo proud?Т the other inquired.

Alice felt even more indignant at this suggestion. СI mean,Т she said, Сthat one canТt help growing older.Т

СOne canТt, perhaps,Т said Humpty Dumpty; Сbut two can. With proper assistance, you might have left off at seven.Т

СWhat a beautiful belt youТve got on!Т Alice suddenly remarked. (They had had quite enough of the subject of age, she thought: and, if they really were to take turns in choosing subjects, it was her turn now.) СAt least,Т she corrected herself on second thoughts, Сa beautiful cravat, I should have said Ц no, a belt, I mean Ц I beg your pardon!Т she added in dismay, for Humpty Dumpty looked thoroughly offended, and she began to wish she hadnТt chosen that subject. Evidently Humpty Dumpty was very angry, though he said nothing for a minute or two. When he did speak again, it was in a deep growl.

СIt is a Ц most Ц provoking Ц thing,Т he said at last, Сwhen a person doesnТt know a cravat from a belt!Т

СItТs a cravat, child, and a beautiful one, as you say. ItТs a present from the White King and Queen. There now!Т

СIs it really?Т said Alice, quite pleased to find that she had chosen a good subject, after all.

СA present given when it isnТt your birthday, of course.Т

Alice considered a little. СI like birthday presents best,Т she said at last.

СAnd how many birthdays have you?Т

СOne.Т

Humpty Dumpty looked doubtful. СIТd rather see that done on paper,Т he said.

Alice couldnТt help smiling as she took out her memorandumbook, and worked the sum for him:








Humpty Dumpty took the book, and looked at it carefully. СThat seems to be done right ЦТ he began.

Chapter VII

The Lion and The Unicorn[76 - The Lion and the Unicorn Ц лев и единорог, символы ¬еликобритании, изображенные на ее гербе, а также персонажи попул€рной песенки, котора€ цитируетс€ в этой главе.]


The next moment soldiers came running through the wood, at first in twos and threes, then ten or twenty together, and at last in such crowds that they seemed to fill the whole forest. Alice got behind a tree, for fear of being run over, and watched them go by.

She thought that in all her life she had never seen soldiers so uncertain on their feet: they were always tripping over something or other, and whenever one went down, several more always fell over him, so that the ground was soon covered with little heaps of men.

Then came the horses. Having four feet, these managed rather better than the footsoldiers: but even they stumbled now and then; and it seemed to be a regular rule that, whenever a horse stumbled, the rider fell off instantly. The confusion got worse every moment, and Alice was very glad to get out of the wood into an open place, where she found the White King seated on the ground, busily writing in his memorandumbook.

СIТve sent them all!Т the King cried in a tone of delight, on seeing Alice. СDid you happen to meet any soldiers, my dear, as you came through the wood?Т

СYes, I did,Т said Alice: Сseveral thousand, I should think.Т

СFour thousand two hundred and seven, thatТs the exact number,Т the King said, referring to his book. СI couldnТt send all the horses, you know, because two of them are wanted in the game. And I havenТt sent the two Messengers, either. TheyТre both gone to the town. Just look along the road, and tell me if you can see either of them.Т

СI see nobody on the road,Т said Alice.

СI only wish I had such eyes,Т the King remarked in a fretful tone. СTo be able to see Nobody! And at that distance, too! Why, itТs as much as I can do to see real people, by this light!Т

All this was lost on Alice, who was still looking intently along the road, shading her eyes with one hand. СI see somebody now!Т she exclaimed at last. СBut heТs coming very slowly Ц and what curious attitudes he goes into!Т (For the Messenger kept skipping up and down, and wriggling like an eel, as he came along, with his great hands spread out like fans on each side.)

СNot at all,Т said the King. СHeТs an AngloSaxon Messenger Ц and those are AngloSaxon attitudes[77 - AngloSaxon attitudes Ц то есть очень замысловато.]. He only does them when heТs happy. His name is Haigha.Т (He pronounced it so as to rhyme with Сmayor.Т)

СI love my love with an H,Т[78 - I love my love with an H Ц это попул€рна€ салонна€ игра ¬икторианской эпохи. ¬се положенные фразы нужно было закончить словами, начинающимис€ с одной буквы, переход€ затем к следующей букве алфавита.] Alice couldnТt help beginning,Т because he is Happy. СHe lives on the Hill,Т the King remarked simply, without the least idea that he was joining in the game, while Alice was still hesitating for the name of a town beginning with H. СThe other MessengerТs called Hatta. I must have two, you know Ц to come and go. Once to come, and one to go.Т

СI beg your pardon?Т said Alice.

СIt isnТt respectable to beg,Т said the King.

СI only meant that I didnТt understand,Т said Alice. СWhy one to come and one to go?Т

СDonТt I tell you?Т the King repeated impatiently. СI must have two Ц to fetch and carry. One to fetch, and one to carry.Т

At this moment the Messenger arrived: he was far too much out of breath to say a word, and could only wave his hands about, and make the most fearful faces at the poor King.

СThis young lady loves you with an H,Т the King said, introducing Alice in the hope of turning off the MessengerТs attention from himself Ц but it was no use Ц the AngloSaxon attitudes only got more extraordinary every moment, while the great eyes rolled wildly from side to side.

СYou alarm me!Т said the King. СI feel faint Ц Give me a ham sandwich!Т

On which the Messenger, to AliceТs great amusement, opened a bag that hung round his neck, and handed a sandwich to the King, who devoured it greedily.

СAnother sandwich!Т said the King.

СThereТs nothing but hay left now,Т the Messenger said, peeping into the bag.

СHay, then,Т the King murmured in a faint whisper.

Alice was glad to see that it revived him a good deal. СThereТs nothing like eating hay when youТre faint,Т he remarked to her, as he munched away.

СI should think throwing cold water over you would be better,Т Alice suggested: СЦor some salvolatile[79 - Salvolatile Ц нюхательна€ соль, смесь на основе нашатырного спирта, которую давали вдохнуть при обмороке.].Т

СI didnТt say there was nothing better,Т the King replied. СI said there was nothing like it.Т Which Alice did not venture to deny.

СWho did you pass on the road?Т the King went on, holding out his hand to the Messenger for some more hay.

СNobody,Т said the Messenger.

СQuite right,Т said the King: Сthis young lady saw him too. So of course Nobody walks slower than you.

СI do my best,Т the Messenger said in a sullen tone. СIТm sure nobody walks much faster than I do!Т

СHe canТt do that,Т said the King, Сor else heТd have been here first. However, now youТve got your breath, you may tell us whatТs happened in the town.Т

СDo you call that a whisper?Т cried the poor King, jumping up and shaking himself. СIf you do such a thing again, IТll have you buttered! It went through and through my head like an earthquake!Т

СIt would have to be a very tiny earthquake!Т thought Alice. СWho are at it again?Т she ventured to ask.

СWhy the Lion and the Unicorn, of course,Т said the King.

СFighting for the crown?Т

		СThe Lion and the Unicorn were fighting for the crown.
		The Lion beat the Unicorn all round the town.
		Some gave them white bread, some gave them brown:
		Some gave them plumcake and drummed them out of town[80 - Drummed them out of town Ц выражение drum smb out of town означает Ђизгнать с позоромї.].Т

СDoes Ц the one Ц that wins Ц get the crown?Т she asked, as well as she could, for the run was putting her quite out of breath.

Alice had no more breath for talking, so the trotted on in silence, till they came in sight of a great crowd, in the middle of which the Lion and Unicorn were fighting. They were in such a cloud of dust, that at first Alice could not make out which was which; but she soon managed to distinguish the Unicorn by his horn.

They placed themselves close to where Hatta, the other Messenger, was standing watching the fight, with a cup of tea in one hand and a piece of breadandbutter in the other.

СHeТs only just out of prison, and he hadnТt finished his tea when he was sent in,Т Haigha whispered to Alice: Сand they only give them oystershells in there Ц so you see heТs very hungry and thirsty. How are you, dear child?Т he went on, putting his arm affectionately round HattaТs neck.

Hatta looked round and nodded, and went on with his bread and butter.

СWere you happy in prison, dear child?Т said Haigha.

Hatta looked round once more, and this time a tear or two trickled down his cheek; but not a word would he say.

СSpeak, canТt you!Т Haigha cried impatiently. But Hatta only munched away, and drank some more tea.

СSpeak, wonТt you!Т cried the King. СHow are they getting on with the fight?Т

Hatta made a desperate effort, and swallowed a large piece of breadandbutter. СTheyТre getting on very well,Т he said in a choking voice: Сeach of them has been down about eightyseven times.Т

СThen I suppose theyТll soon bring the white bread and the brown?Т Alice ventured to remark.

СItТs waiting for Сem now,Т said Hatta; Сthis is a bit of it as IТm eating.Т

There was a pause in the fight just then, and the Lion and the Unicorn sat down, panting, while the King called out СTen minutes allowed for refreshments!Т Haigha and Hatta set to work at once, carrying round trays of white and brown bread. Alice took a piece to taste, but it was very dry.

СI donТt think theyТll fight any more today,Т the King said to Hatta: Сgo and order the drums to begin.Т And Hatta went bounding away like a grasshopper.

For a minute or two Alice stood silent, watching him. Suddenly she brightened up. СLook, look!Т she cried, pointing eagerly. СThereТs the White Queen running across the country! She came flying out of the wood over yonder Ц Ц How fast those Queens can run!Т

СThereТs some enemy after, her no doubt,Т the King said, without even looking round. СThat woodТs full of them.Т

СBut arenТt you going to run and help her?Т Alice asked, very much surprised at his taking it so quietly.

СNo use, no use!Т said the King. СShe runs so fearfully quick. You might as well try to catch a Bandersnatch! But IТll make a memorandum about her, if you like Ц Ц SheТs a dear good creature,Т he repeated softly to himself, as he opened his memorandumbook. СDo you spell УcreatureФ with a double УeФ?Т

At this moment the Unicorn sauntered by them, with his hands in his pockets. СI had the best of it this time?Т he said to the King, just glancing at him as he passed.

СA little Ц a little,Т the King replied, rather nervously. СYou shouldnТt have run him through with your horn, you know.Т

СIt didnТt hurt him,Т the Unicorn said carelessly, and he was going on, when his eye happened to fall upon Alice: he turned round rather instantly, and stood for some time looking at her with an air of the deepest disgust.

СWhat Ц is Ц this?Т he said at last.

СThis is a child!Т Haigha replied eagerly, coming in front of Alice to introduce her, and spreading out both his hands towards her in an AngloSaxon attitude. СWe only found it today. ItТs as large as life, and twice as natural!Т

СI always thought they were fabulous monsters!Т said the Unicorn. СIs at alive?Т

СIt can talk,Т said Haigha solemnly.

The Unicorn looked dreamily at Alice, and said СTalk, child.Т

Alice could not help her lips curing up into a smile as she began: СDo you know, I always thought Unicorns were fabulous monsters, too? I never saw one alive before!Т

СWell, now that we have seen each other,Т said the Unicorn, Сif youТll believe in me, IТll believe in you. Is that a bargain?Т

СYes, if you like,Т said Alice.

СCome, fetch out the plumcake, old man!Т the Unicorn went on, turning from her to the King. СNone of your brown bread for me!Т

СCertainly Ц certainly!Т the King muttered, and beckoned to Haigha. СOpen the bag!Т he whispered. СQuick! Not that one Ц thatТs full of hay!Т

Haigha took a large cake out of the bag, and gave it to Alice to hold, while he got out a dish and carvingknife. How they all came out of it Alice couldnТt guess. It was just like a conjuringtrick[81 - Conjuringtrick Ц фокус.], she thought.

The Lion had joined them while this was going on: he looked very tired and sleepy, and his eyes were half shut. СWhatТs this!Т he said, blinking lazily at Alice, and speaking in a deep hollow tone that sounded like the tolling of a great bell.

СAh, what is it, now?Т the Unicorn cried eagerly. СYouТll never guess! I couldnТt.Т

The Lion looked at Alice wearily. СAre you animal Ц vegetable Ц or mineral?Т he said, yawning at every other word.

СItТs a fabulous monster!Т the Unicorn cried out, before Alice could reply.

СThen hand round the plumcake, Monster,Т the Lion said, lying down and putting his chin on his paws. СAnd sit down, both of you,Т (to the King and the Unicorn): Сfair play with the cake, you know!Т

The King was evidently very uncomfortable at having to sit down between the two great creatures; but there was no other place for him.

СWhat a fight we might have for the crown, now!Т the Unicorn said, looking slyly up at the crown, which the poor King was nearly shaking off his head, he trembled so much.

СI should win easy,Т said the Lion.

СIТm not so sure of that,Т said the Unicorn.

СWhy, I beat you all round the town, you chicken[82 - Chicken Ц трус.]!Т the Lion replied angrily, half getting up as he spoke.

Here the King interrupted, to prevent the quarrel going on: he was very nervous, and his voice quite quivered. СAll round the town?Т he said. СThatТs a good long way. Did you go by the old bridge, or the marketplace? You get the best view by the old bridge.Т

СIТm sure I donТt know,Т the Lion growled out as he lay down again. СThere was too much dust to see anything. What a time the Monster is, cutting up that cake!Т

Alice had seated herself on the bank of a little brook, with the great dish on her knees, and was sawing away diligently with the knife. СItТs very provoking!Т she said, in reply to the Lion (she was getting quite used to being called Сthe MonsterТ). СIТve cut several slices already, but they always join on again!Т

СYou donТt know how to manage Lookingglass cakes,Т the Unicorn remarked. СHand it round first, and cut it afterwards.Т

This sounded nonsense, but Alice very obediently got up, and carried the dish round, and the cake divided itself into three pieces as she did so. СNow cut it up,Т said the Lion, as she returned to her place with the empty dish.

СI say, this isnТt fair!Т cried the Unicorn, as Alice sat with the knife in her hand, very much puzzled how to begin. СThe Monster has given the Lion twice as much as me!Т

СSheТs kept none for herself, anyhow,Т said the Lion. СDo you like plumcake, Monster?Т

But before Alice could answer him, the drums began.

Where the noise came from, she couldnТt make out: the air seemed full of it, and it rang through and through her head till she felt quite deafened. She started to her feet and sprang across the little brook in her terror,


* * *

and had just time to see the Lion and the Unicorn rise to their feet, with angry looks at being interrupted in their feast, before she dropped to her knees, and put her hands over her ears, vainly trying to shut out the dreadful uproar.

СIf that doesnТt Уdrum them out of town,ФТ she thought to herself, Сnothing ever will!Т




Chapter VIII

СItТs My Own InventionТ


After a while the noise seemed gradually to die away, till all was dead silence, and Alice lifted up her head in some alarm. There was no one to be seen, and her first thought was that she must have been dreaming about the Lion and the Unicorn and those queer AngloSaxon Messengers. However, there was the great dish still lying at her feet, on which she had tried to cut the plumcake, СSo I wasnТt dreaming, after all,Т she said to herself, Сunless Ц unless weТre all part of the same dream. Only I do hope itТs my dream, and not the Red KingТs! I donТt like belonging to another personТs dream,Т she went on in a rather complaining tone: СIТve a great mind to go and wake him, and see what happens!Т

At this moment her thoughts were interrupted by a loud shouting of СAhoy![83 - Ahoy! Ц устаревший способ окликнуть когол., часто используетс€ в юмористическом контексте.] Ahoy! Check![84 - Check Ц шах (угроза королю противника).]Т and a Knight, dressed in crimson armour, came galloping down upon her, brandishing a great club. Just as he reached her, the horse stopped suddenly: СYouТre my prisoner!Т the Knight cried, as he tumbled off his horse.

Startled as she was, Alice was more frightened for him than for herself at the moment, and watched him with some anxiety as he mounted again. As soon as he was comfortably in the saddle, he began once more СYouТre myЦТ but here another voice broke in СAhoy! Ahoy! Check!Т and Alice looked round in some surprise for the new enemy.

This time it was a White Knight. He drew up at AliceТs side, and tumbled off his horse just as the Red Knight had done: then he got on again, and the two Knights sat and looked at each other for some time without speaking. Alice looked from one to the other in some bewilderment.

СSheТs my prisoner, you know!Т the Red Knight said at last.

СYes, but then I came and rescued her!Т the White Knight replied.

СWell, we must fight for her, then,Т said the Red Knight, as he took up his helmet (which hung from the saddle, and was something the shape of a horseТs head) and put it on.

СYou will observe the Rules of Battle, of course?Т the White Knight remarked, putting on his helmet too.

СI always do,Т said the Red Knight, and they began banging away at each other with such fury that Alice got behind a tree to be out of the way of the blows.

СI wonder, now, what the Rules of Battle are,Т she said to herself, as she watched the fight, timidly peeping out from her hidingplace: СOne Rule seems to be, that if one Knight hits the other, he knocks him off his horse; and if he misses, he tumbles off himself Ц and another Rule seems to be that they hold their clubs with their arms, as if they were Punch and Judy Ц What a noise they make when they tumble! Just like a whole set of fireirons falling into the fender! And how quiet the horses are! They let them get on and off them just as if they were tables!Т

Another Rule of Battle, that Alice had not noticed, seemed to be that they always fell on their heads; and the battle ended with their both falling off in this way, side by side. When they got up again, they shook hands, and then the Red Knight mounted and galloped off.

СIt was a glorious victory, wasnТt it?Т said the White Knight, as he came up panting.

СSo you will, when youТve crossed the next brook,Т said the White Knight. СIТll see you safe to the end of the wood Ц and then I must go back, you know. ThatТs the end of my move.Т

СThank you very much,Т said Alice. СMay I help you off with your helmet?Т It was evidently more than he could manage by himself: however, she managed to shake him out of it at last.

СNow one can breathe more easily,Т said the Knight, putting back his shaggy hair with both hands, and turning his gentle face and large mild eyes to Alice.[85 - »сследователи утверждают, что это описание самого Ћьюиса  эрролла.  артину дополн€ют живое воображение рыцар€, его неуклюжие ужимки и неспособность сопровождать јлису на ее пути в королевы.] She thought she had never seen such a strangelooking soldier in all her life.

He was dressed in tin armour, which seemed to fit him very badly, and he had a queershaped little deal box fastened across his shoulder, upsidedown, and with the lid hanging open. Alice looked at it with great curiosity.

СI see youТre admiring my little box,Т the Knight said in a friendly tone. СItТs my own invention Ц to keep clothes and sandwiches in. You see I carry it upsidedown, so that the rain canТt get in.Т

СCan you guess why I did that?Т he said to Alice.

Alice shook her head.

СIn hopes some bees may make a nest in it Ц then I should get the honey.Т

It didnТt sound a comfortable plan, Alice thought, and for a few minutes she walked on in silence, puzzling over the idea, and every now and then stopping to help the poor Knight, who certainly was not a good rider.

Whenever the horse stopped (which it did very often), he fell off in front; and, whenever it went on again (which it generally did rather suddenly), he fell off behind. Otherwise he kept on pretty well, except that he had a habit of now and then falling off sideways; and, as he generally did this on the side on which Alice was walking, she soon found that it was the best plan not to walk quite close to the horse.

СIТm afraid youТve not had much practice in riding,Т she ventured to say, as she was helping him up from his fifth tumble.

The Knight looked very much surprised, and a little offended at the remark. СWhat makes you say that?Т he asked, as he scrambled back into the saddle, keeping hold of AliceТs hair with one hand, to save himself from falling over on the other side.

СBecause people donТt fall off quite so often, when theyТve had much practice.Т

СThe great art of riding,Т the Knight suddenly began in a loud voice, waving his right arm as he spoke, Сis to keep ЦТ Here the sentence ended as suddenly as it had begun, as the Knight fell heavily on the top of his head exactly in the path were Alice was walking. She was quite frightened this time, and said in an anxious tone, as she picked him up, СI hope no bones are broken?Т

Of all the strange things that Alice saw in her journey Through The LookingGlass, this was the one that she always remembered most clearly. Years afterwards she could bring the whole scene back again, as if it had been only yesterday Ц the mild blue eyes and kindly smile of the Knight Ц the setting sun gleaming through his hair, and shining on his armour in a blaze of light that quite dazzled her Ц the horse quietly moving about, with the reins hanging loose on his neck, cropping the grass at her feet Ц and the black shadows of the forest behind Ц all this she took in like a picture, as, with one hand shading her eyes, she leant against a tree, watching the strange pair, and listening, in a half dream, to the melancholy music of the song.

* * *

and threw herself down to rest on a lawn as soft as moss, with little flowerbeds dotted about it here and there. СOh, how glad I am to get here! And what is this on my head?Т she exclaimed in a tone of dismay, as she put her hands up to something very heavy, that fitted tight all round her head.

СBut how can it have got there without my knowing it?Т she said to herself, as she lifted it off, and set it on her lap to make out what it could possibly be.

It was a golden crown.




Chapter IX

Queen Alice


СWell, this is grand!Т said Alice. СI never expected I should be a Queen so soon Ц and IТll tell you what it is, your Majesty,Т she went on in a severe tone (she was always rather fond of scolding herself), СitТll never do for you to be lolling about on the grass like that! Queens have to be dignified, you know!Т

So she got up and walked about Ц rather stiffly just at first, as she was afraid that the crown might come off: but she comforted herself with the thought that there was nobody to see her, Сand if I really am a Queen,Т she said as she sat down again, СI shall be able to manage it quite well in time.Т

Everything was happening so oddly that she didnТt feel a bit surprised at finding the Red Queen and the White Queen sitting close to her, one on each side: she would have liked very much to ask them how they came there, but she feared it would not be quite civil. However, there would be no harm, she thought, in asking if the game was over. СPlease, would you tell me ЦТ she began, looking timidly at the Red Queen.

СSpeak when youТre spoken to!Т[92 - Ђ√оворить, только когда к тебе обращаютс€ї, было незыблемым правилом детей ¬икторианской эпохи.] The Queen sharply interrupted her.

СBut if everybody obeyed that rule,Т said Alice, who was always ready for a little argument, Сand if you only spoke when you were spoken to, and the other person always waited for you to begin, you see nobody would ever say anything, so that ЦТ

СRidiculous!Т cried the Queen. СWhy, donТt you see, child ЦТ here she broke off with a frown, and, after thinking for a minute, suddenly changed the subject of the conversation. СWhat do you mean by УIf you really are a QueenФ? What right have you to call yourself so? You canТt be a Queen, you know, till youТve passed the proper examination. And the sooner we begin it, the better.Т

СI only said УifФ!Т poor Alice pleaded in a piteous tone.

The two Queens looked at each other, and the Red Queen remarked, with a little shudder, СShe says she only said УifФ ЦТ

СBut she said a great deal more than that!Т the White Queen moaned, wringing her hands. СOh, ever so much more than that!Т

СSo you did, you know,Т the Red Queen said to Alice. СAlways speak the truth Ц think before you speak Ц and write it down afterwards.Т

СIТm sure I didnТt mean ЦТ Alice was beginning, but the Red Queen interrupted her impatiently.

СThatТs just what I complain of! You should have meant! What do you suppose is the use of child without any meaning? Even a joke should have some meaning Ц and a childТs more important than a joke, I hope. You couldnТt deny that, even if you tried with both hands.Т

СI donТt deny things with my hands,Т Alice objected.

СNobody said you did,Т said the Red Queen. СI said you couldnТt if you tried.Т

СSheТs in that state of mind,Т said the White Queen, Сthat she wants to deny something Ц only she doesnТt know what to deny!Т

СA nasty, vicious temper,Т the Red Queen remarked; and then there was an uncomfortable silence for a minute or two.

The Red Queen broke the silence by saying to the White Queen, СI invite you to AliceТs dinnerparty this afternoon.Т

The White Queen smiled feebly, and said СAnd I invite you.Т

СI didnТt know I was to have a party at all,Т said Alice; Сbut if there is to be one, I think I ought to invite the guests.Т

СWe gave you the opportunity of doing it,Т the Red Queen remarked: Сbut I daresay youТve not had many lessons in manners yet?Т

СManners are not taught in lessons,Т said Alice. СLessons teach you to do sums, and things of that sort.Т

СAnd you do Addition?Т the White Queen asked. СWhatТs one and one and one and one and one and one and one and one and one and one?Т

СI donТt know,Т said Alice. СI lost count.Т

СShe canТt do Addition,Т the Red Queen interrupted. СCan you do Subtraction? Take nine from eight.Т

СNine from eight I canТt, you know,Т Alice replied very readily: Сbut ЦТ

СShe canТt do Subtraction,Т said the White Queen. СCan you do Division? Divide a loaf by a knife Ц whatТs the answer to that?Т

СI suppose ЦТ Alice was beginning, but the Red Queen answered for her. СBreadandbutter, of course. Try another Subtraction sum. Take a bone from a dog: what remains?Т

Alice considered. СThe bone wouldnТt remain, of course, if I took it Ц and the dog wouldnТt remain; it would come to bite me Ц and IТm sure I shouldnТt remain!Т

СThen you think nothing would remain?Т said the Red Queen.

СI think thatТs the answer.Т

СWrong, as usual,Т said the Red Queen: Сthe dogТs temper would remain.Т

СBut I donТt see how ЦТ

СWhy, look here!Т the Red Queen cried. СThe dog would lose its temper, wouldnТt it?Т

СPerhaps it would,Т Alice replied cautiously.

СThen if the dog went away, its temper would remain!Т the Queen exclaimed triumphantly.

Alice said, as gravely as she could, СThey might go different ways.Т But she couldnТt help thinking to herself СWhat dreadful nonsense we are talking!Т

And then (as Alice afterwards described it) all sorts of thing happened in a moment. The candles all grew up to the ceiling, looking something like a bed of rushes with fireworks at the top. At any other time, Alice would have felt surprised at this, but she was far too much excited to be surprised at anything now. СAs for you,Т she repeated, catching hold of the little creature in the very act of jumping over a bottle which had just lighted upon the table, СIТll shake you into a kitten, that I will!Т




Chapter X

Shaking


She took her off the table as she spoke, and shook her backwards and forwards with all her might.

The Red Queen made no resistance whatever: only her face grew very small, and her eyes got large and green: and still, as Alice went on shaking her, she kept on growing shorter Ц and fatter Ц and softer Ц and rounder Ц and Ц

Chapter XII

Which Dreamed It?


СYour Red Majesty shouldnТt purr so loud,Т Alice said, rubbing her eyes, and addressing the kitten, respectfully, yet with some severity. СYou woke me out of oh! such a nice dream! And youТve been along with me, Kitty Ц all through the LookingGlass world. Did you know it, dear?Т

It is a very inconvenient habit of kittens (Alice had once made the remark) that, whatever you say to them, they always purr. СIf they would only purr for УyesФ and mew for Уno,Ф or any rule of that sort,Т she had said, Сso that one could keep up a conversation! But how can you talk with a person if they always say the same thing?Т

On this occasion the kitten only purred: and it was impossible to guess whether it meant СyesТ or Сno.Т

So Alice hunted among the chessmen on the table till she had found the Red Queen: then she went down on her knees on the hearthrug, and put the kitten and the Queen to look at each other. СNow, Kitty!Т she cried, clapping her hands triumphantly. СConfess that was what you turned into!Т

(СBut it wouldnТt look at it,Т she said, when she was explaining the thing afterwards to her sister: Сit turned away its head, and pretended not to see it: but it looked a little ashamed of itself, so I think it must have been the Red Queen.Т)

СSit up a little more stiffly, dear!Т Alice cried with a merry laugh. СAnd curtsey while youТre thinking what to Ц what to purr. It saves time, remember!Т And she caught it up and gave it one little kiss, Сjust in honour of having been a Red Queen.Т

СSnowdrop, my pet!Т she went on, looking over her shoulder at the White Kitten, which was still patiently undergoing its toilet, Сwhen will Dinah have finished with your White Majesty, I wonder? That must be the reason you were so untidy in my dream Ц Dinah! do you know that youТre scrubbing a White Queen? Really, itТs most disrespectful of you!

СAnd what did Dinah turn to, I wonder?Т she prattled on, as she settled comfortably down, with one elbow in the rug, and her chin in her hand, to watch the kittens. СTell me, Dinah, did you turn to Humpty Dumpty? I think you did Ц however, youТd better not mention it to your friends just yet, for IТm not sure.

СBy the way, Kitty, if only youТd been really with me in my dream, there was one thing you would have enjoyed Ц I had such a quantity of poetry said to me, all about fishes! Tomorrow morning you shall have a real treat. All the time youТre eating your breakfast, IТll repeat УThe Walrus and the CarpenterФ to you; and then you can make believe itТs oysters, dear!

СNow, Kitty, letТs consider who it was that dreamed it all. This is a serious question, my dear, and you should not go on licking your paw like that Ц as if Dinah hadnТt washed you this morning! You see, Kitty, it must have been either me or the Red King. He was part of my dream, of course Ц but then I was part of his dream, too! Was it the Red King, Kitty? You were his wife, my dear, so you ought to know Ц Ц Oh, Kitty, do help to settle it! IТm sure your paw can wait!Т But the provoking kitten only began on the other paw, and pretended it hadnТt heard the question.

Which do you think it was?
